Times News Service  No. 1631 Fri., June 9 (NYLite items can be published if desired; please credit the contributor. Contribute to NYLite and get a NYT rolling-writer style pen.)  DECOY OWL DEPLOYED IN CRACKDOWN ON PIGEONS IN TIMES SQUARE  NEW YORK _ With a 25-pound stone owl, summons books and lots of dis _ as in disrespect and disdain _ New York City opened a new offensive Thursday against an old foe: the pigeon.  At the outset of Operation Stool Pigeon, as the campaign was called by the Department of Sanitation, the objective of the land-based forces was to reclaim Times Square from the squadrons of birds that have defaced signs and sidewalks with their droppings, and to interdict their supply lines by warning pigeon feeders to cease and desist or face fines as high as $250.  The Sanitation Department instructed its enforcement officers to focus on areas favored by the pigeons and their fanciers _ 42nd Street between Broadway and Eighth Avenue and the medians between Broadway and Seventh Avenue from 43rd to 46th Streets.  The Times Square Business Improvement District, which joined in the effort, deployed its faux owl _ real owls being pigeon predators _ in a planter between 45th and 46th Streets on Broadway, to convince the birds that they could get mugged in Times Square.  Giving the history behind Operation Stool Pigeon, Lucian Chalfen, a spokesman for the Department of Sanitation, said:  ``Basically, the overall complaint was that there seems to have been a proliferation of pigeons in the general Times Square area directly attributable to the fact that there are people feeding these birds. And I guess it gets around in bird circles. A nominal problem all around the city and in many cities exacerbated itself in that area.''  Christine Sheppard, curator of birds at the Bronx Zoo, said that when birds that normally go exploring for food find a good source, they have no reason to look elsewhere: ``The number of animals that come to this party continues to increase, and nobody leaves.''  Dr. Sheppard held out scant hope for the efficacy of the stone owl. ``Hilarious,'' she said. ``These birds are not stupid. In order to survive in the wild, pigeons have to be aware of predators. Any bird that sits still for 24, 48, 96 hours they know is not dangerous.''  _NEW YORK TIMES NEWS SERVICE  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Mickey Mantle's prognosis is described as excellent. Another home run would be nice, but we bet he'd settle for a home walk.  Forecasters expect several major hurricanes this season. This will be disappointing to folks hoping to keep the high winds, damage and all-wet people confined to Washington.  ``We are on the verge of having a 40-year low in the minimum wage,'' Mr. Clinton says. And who knows which office-holder after 1996 will be forced to get a job paying that?  ``Everybody wants to cut Medicaid to shreds,'' the president says. Not so. Some folks want to cut it to smaller pieces than shreds.  The man at the next desk says you know it's a bad day when you fax an e-mail to someone's voice mail but it gets detoured through the copier and into the shredder.  Newt Gingrich is going to do a cross-country tour to sell his new book, ``To Renew America.'' A cross country. That pretty well describes the U.S. now.  Any day now we expect to see an official ballot that lists the candidates' names followed by a Universal Price Code for each.  A new survey shows coin collectors would like to see Ronald Reagan's face on a proposed new dollar coin. Because they admire his mettle?  ``The mining law of 1872 needs to be modernized,'' says Mr. Clinton. Though he failed to suggest who should get the shaft.  ``Farming,'' Mr. Clinton says, ``has become more and more capital-intensive.'' It's one more way farming resembles life.  IS THERE INTELLIGENT LIFE ELSEWHERE IN THE COSMOS? DON'T BET ON IT (Bonus NYLite column) By BILL TAMMEUS  Admit it. When you look into the night sky (assuming you can see anything past the urban light pollution and this spring's incessant clouds), don't you wonder if there are other people on other planets?  Naturally you do. It's simple curiosity. Besides, it takes your mind off the O.J. Simpson trial, Bosnia, the Michigan Militia and the start any day now of the pro football season.  Of course, our collective hope that we're not alone in the universe is tempered by a fear that aliens from other planets may be no better than we are _ may, in fact, be waiting for a dark night to mug Earth and steal her purse.  Despite such fears, our insatiable drive to learn whether we're alone in the universe is causing space scientists to ask Congress for money to conduct a search for other planets in the galaxy.  Well, it's either that or NASA's insatiable drive to survive as a government agency at a time when Congress is sharpening its budget axes. (I stole that axes cliche directly from the last 3,739 wire stories about Congress and the budget. So sue me.)  Anyway, NASA's idea, as I get it, is to check out some of the Milky Way's 100 billion or so stars with various telescopes to see if there are some that might have solar systems. If they find some, they would send unpersoned spacecraft to the area to see if there really were planets.  The proof of planetary life elsewhere, I suppose, would be getting one of these space probes back stamped ``Return to Sender.''  NASA, as you may know, is not the only group interested in finding other life out in the universe.  Scientists from the SETI Institute in Mountain View, Calif., (SETI, pronounced SETI, stands for Search for Extraterrestrial Intelligence) recently set up a computerized listening system in Australia to pick up sounds of other life. SETI has been in existence for more than 30 years. So, in fact, have I, but let it go.  And amateur groups all over the world have been collecting data and rumors on UFOs and on aliens who may or may not have visited Earth, may or may not have borrowed some of us for experimental purposes and may or may not be waiting on us in fast-food franchises.  I frankly don't know why we're so fascinated by the possibility of life elsewhere. My guess is we wouldn't be if we were happy with the life forms around us.  But we look out our windows and see lumpy people and skinny people and people with earrings in their navels. And we say to ourselves, ``Is this the best we can do?'' Then we look in the mirror and say the same thing.  Which causes us to wander out into the night, stare at the sky and imagine beautiful people with strikingly high intelligence living on some distant planet _ people who never cut their nails in public, who never order the wrong wine with dinner, who always manage to balance their checkbooks.  But my guess is that if NASA convinces Congress to fund its search for life and if we eventually find other people, we're going to be disappointed.  What, after all, ever matches our dreams? Well, I mean besides the time I dreamed I was drowning and woke up with my head sticking out of a tent in the rain.  Probably the people we find, if any, will want to lend us money at exorbitant rates. Or sell us second-quality cars at first-quality prices. Or borrow our mowers and not return them. Shoot, for people like that we can limit our search to a couple of ZIP Codes I could name but won't. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950609.0355 type=story > <HEADLINE> MOYNIHAN AIDE COMES UNDER FIRE FOR COMMUTING, HIRING PRACTICES </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> WASHINGTON (BC-MOYNIHAN-AIDE-HNS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199506.gz"
"24634"	"I"	"suppose"	" Her late husband's mother is a member and plays in the Women's Golf Association of Massachusetts tournaments. ``But she is retired and can play in their weekday tournaments,'' said Bowes. ``I have to work and can't.'' </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950609.0379 type=other > <DATELINE>  (BC-NYLITE(SMILE)-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> c.1995 N.Y. But my guess is that if NASA convinces Congress to fund its search for life and if we eventually find other people, we're going to be disappointed.  What, after all, ever matches our dreams? Well, I mean besides the time I dreamed I was drowning and woke up with my head sticking out of a tent in the rain.  Probably the people we find, if any, will want to lend us money at exorbitant rates. Or sell us second-quality cars at first-quality prices. Or borrow our mowers and not return them. Shoot, for people like that we can limit our search to a couple of ZIP Codes I could name but won't. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950609.0383 type=other > <DATELINE>  (BC-NYLITE(SMILE)-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  No. 1631 Fri., June 9 (NYLite items can be published if desired; please credit the contributor. Contribute to NYLite and get a NYT rolling-writer style pen.)  DECOY OWL DEPLOYED IN CRACKDOWN ON PIGEONS IN TIMES SQUARE  NEW YORK _ With a 25-pound stone owl, summons books and lots of dis _ as in disrespect and disdain _ New York City opened a new offensive Thursday against an old foe: the pigeon.  At the outset of Operation Stool Pigeon, as the campaign was called by the Department of Sanitation, the objective of the land-based forces was to reclaim Times Square from the squadrons of birds that have defaced signs and sidewalks with their droppings, and to interdict their supply lines by warning pigeon feeders to cease and desist or face fines as high as $250.  The Sanitation Department instructed its enforcement officers to focus on areas favored by the pigeons and their fanciers _ 42nd Street between Broadway and Eighth Avenue and the medians between Broadway and Seventh Avenue from 43rd to 46th Streets.  The Times Square Business Improvement District, which joined in the effort, deployed its faux owl _ real owls being pigeon predators _ in a planter between 45th and 46th Streets on Broadway, to convince the birds that they could get mugged in Times Square.  Giving the history behind Operation Stool Pigeon, Lucian Chalfen, a spokesman for the Department of Sanitation, said:  ``Basically, the overall complaint was that there seems to have been a proliferation of pigeons in the general Times Square area directly attributable to the fact that there are people feeding these birds. And I guess it gets around in bird circles. A nominal problem all around the city and in many cities exacerbated itself in that area.''  Christine Sheppard, curator of birds at the Bronx Zoo, said that when birds that normally go exploring for food find a good source, they have no reason to look elsewhere: ``The number of animals that come to this party continues to increase, and nobody leaves.''  Dr. Sheppard held out scant hope for the efficacy of the stone owl. ``Hilarious,'' she said. ``These birds are not stupid. In order to survive in the wild, pigeons have to be aware of predators. Any bird that sits still for 24, 48, 96 hours they know is not dangerous.''  _NEW YORK TIMES NEWS SERVICE  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Mickey Mantle's prognosis is described as excellent. Another home run would be nice, but we bet he'd settle for a home walk.  Forecasters expect several major hurricanes this season. This will be disappointing to folks hoping to keep the high winds, damage and all-wet people confined to Washington.  ``We are on the verge of having a 40-year low in the minimum wage,'' Mr. Clinton says. And who knows which office-holder after 1996 will be forced to get a job paying that?  ``Everybody wants to cut Medicaid to shreds,'' the president says. Not so. Some folks want to cut it to smaller pieces than shreds.  The man at the next desk says you know it's a bad day when you fax an e-mail to someone's voice mail but it gets detoured through the copier and into the shredder.  Newt Gingrich is going to do a cross-country tour to sell his new book, ``To Renew America.'' A cross country. That pretty well describes the U.S. now.  Any day now we expect to see an official ballot that lists the candidates' names followed by a Universal Price Code for each.  A new survey shows coin collectors would like to see Ronald Reagan's face on a proposed new dollar coin. Because they admire his mettle?  ``The mining law of 1872 needs to be modernized,'' says Mr. Clinton. Though he failed to suggest who should get the shaft.  ``Farming,'' Mr. Clinton says, ``has become more and more capital-intensive.'' It's one more way farming resembles life.  IS THERE INTELLIGENT LIFE ELSEWHERE IN THE COSMOS? DON'T BET ON IT (Bonus NYLite column) By BILL TAMMEUS  Admit it. When you look into the night sky (assuming you can see anything past the urban light pollution and this spring's incessant clouds), don't you wonder if there are other people on other planets?  Naturally you do. It's simple curiosity. Besides, it takes your mind off the O.J. Simpson trial, Bosnia, the Michigan Militia and the start any day now of the pro football season.  Of course, our collective hope that we're not alone in the universe is tempered by a fear that aliens from other planets may be no better than we are _ may, in fact, be waiting for a dark night to mug Earth and steal her purse.  Despite such fears, our insatiable drive to learn whether we're alone in the universe is causing space scientists to ask Congress for money to conduct a search for other planets in the galaxy.  Well, it's either that or NASA's insatiable drive to survive as a government agency at a time when Congress is sharpening its budget axes. (I stole that axes cliche directly from the last 3,739 wire stories about Congress and the budget. So sue me.)  Anyway, NASA's idea, as I get it, is to check out some of the Milky Way's 100 billion or so stars with various telescopes to see if there are some that might have solar systems. If they find some, they would send unpersoned spacecraft to the area to see if there really were planets.  The proof of planetary life elsewhere, I suppose, would be getting one of these space probes back stamped ``Return to Sender.''  NASA, as you may know, is not the only group interested in finding other life out in the universe.  Scientists from the SETI Institute in Mountain View, Calif., (SETI, pronounced SETI, stands for Search for Extraterrestrial Intelligence) recently set up a computerized listening system in Australia to pick up sounds of other life. SETI has been in existence for more than 30 years. So, in fact, have I, but let it go.  And amateur groups all over the world have been collecting data and rumors on UFOs and on aliens who may or may not have visited Earth, may or may not have borrowed some of us for experimental purposes and may or may not be waiting on us in fast-food franchises.  I frankly don't know why we're so fascinated by the possibility of life elsewhere. My guess is we wouldn't be if we were happy with the life forms around us.  But we look out our windows and see lumpy people and skinny people and people with earrings in their navels. And we say to ourselves, ``Is this the best we can do?'' Then we look in the mirror and say the same thing.  Which causes us to wander out into the night, stare at the sky and imagine beautiful people with strikingly high intelligence living on some distant planet _ people who never cut their nails in public, who never order the wrong wine with dinner, who always manage to balance their checkbooks.  But my guess is that if NASA convinces Congress to fund its search for life and if we eventually find other people, we're going to be disappointed.  What, after all, ever matches our dreams? Well, I mean besides the time I dreamed I was drowning and woke up with my head sticking out of a tent in the rain.  Probably the people we find, if any, will want to lend us money at exorbitant rates. Or sell us second-quality cars at first-quality prices. Or borrow our mowers and not return them. Shoot, for people like that we can limit our search to a couple of ZIP Codes I could name but won't. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950609.0384 type=story > <HEADLINE> COMMENTARY: ALL IN WHAT FAMILY? </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-BAKER-COLUMN-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199506.gz"
"24636"	"I"	"suppose"	" ``This is something everyone has a stake in,'' he said. ``You can't just go to the elderly. You have to ask all generations what they want to do. You really need a sense of where the American people want to go with this.'' </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950609.0387 type=other > <DATELINE>  (BC-NYLITE(SMILE)-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> c.1995 N.Y. Though he failed to suggest who should get the shaft.  ``Farming,'' Mr. Clinton says, ``has become more and more capital-intensive.'' It's one more way farming resembles life.  IS THERE INTELLIGENT LIFE ELSEWHERE IN THE COSMOS? DON'T BET ON IT (Bonus NYLite column) By BILL TAMMEUS  Admit it. When you look into the night sky (assuming you can see anything past the urban light pollution and this spring's incessant clouds), don't you wonder if there are other people on other planets?  Naturally you do. It's simple curiosity. Besides, it takes your mind off the O.J. Simpson trial, Bosnia, the Michigan Militia and the start any day now of the pro football season.  Of course, our collective hope that we're not alone in the universe is tempered by a fear that aliens from other planets may be no better than we are _ may, in fact, be waiting for a dark night to mug Earth and steal her purse.  Despite such fears, our insatiable drive to learn whether we're alone in the universe is causing space scientists to ask Congress for money to conduct a search for other planets in the galaxy.  Well, it's either that or NASA's insatiable drive to survive as a government agency at a time when Congress is sharpening its budget axes. (I stole that axes cliche directly from the last 3,739 wire stories about Congress and the budget. So sue me.)  Anyway, NASA's idea, as I get it, is to check out some of the Milky Way's 100 billion or so stars with various telescopes to see if there are some that might have solar systems. If they find some, they would send unpersoned spacecraft to the area to see if there really were planets.  The proof of planetary life elsewhere, I suppose, would be getting one of these space probes back stamped ``Return to Sender.''  NASA, as you may know, is not the only group interested in finding other life out in the universe.  Scientists from the SETI Institute in Mountain View, Calif., (SETI, pronounced SETI, stands for Search for Extraterrestrial Intelligence) recently set up a computerized listening system in Australia to pick up sounds of other life. SETI has been in existence for more than 30 years. So, in fact, have I, but let it go.  And amateur groups all over the world have been collecting data and rumors on UFOs and on aliens who may or may not have visited Earth, may or may not have borrowed some of us for experimental purposes and may or may not be waiting on us in fast-food franchises.  I frankly don't know why we're so fascinated by the possibility of life elsewhere. My guess is we wouldn't be if we were happy with the life forms around us.  But we look out our windows and see lumpy people and skinny people and people with earrings in their navels. And we say to ourselves, ``Is this the best we can do?'' Then we look in the mirror and say the same thing.  Which causes us to wander out into the night, stare at the sky and imagine beautiful people with strikingly high intelligence living on some distant planet _ people who never cut their nails in public, who never order the wrong wine with dinner, who always manage to balance their checkbooks.  But my guess is that if NASA convinces Congress to fund its search for life and if we eventually find other people, we're going to be disappointed.  What, after all, ever matches our dreams? Well, I mean besides the time I dreamed I was drowning and woke up with my head sticking out of a tent in the rain.  Probably the people we find, if any, will want to lend us money at exorbitant rates. Or sell us second-quality cars at first-quality prices. Or borrow our mowers and not return them. Shoot, for people like that we can limit our search to a couple of ZIP Codes I could name but won't. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950610.0157 type=other > <DATELINE>  (BC-NYLITE(SMILE)-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  No. 1631 Fri., June 9 (NYLite items can be published if desired; please credit the contributor. Contribute to NYLite and get a NYT rolling-writer style pen.)  DECOY OWL DEPLOYED IN CRACKDOWN ON PIGEONS IN TIMES SQUARE  NEW YORK _ With a 25-pound stone owl, summons books and lots of dis _ as in disrespect and disdain _ New York City opened a new offensive Thursday against an old foe: the pigeon.  At the outset of Operation Stool Pigeon, as the campaign was called by the Department of Sanitation, the objective of the land-based forces was to reclaim Times Square from the squadrons of birds that have defaced signs and sidewalks with their droppings, and to interdict their supply lines by warning pigeon feeders to cease and desist or face fines as high as $250.  The Sanitation Department instructed its enforcement officers to focus on areas favored by the pigeons and their fanciers _ 42nd Street between Broadway and Eighth Avenue and the medians between Broadway and Seventh Avenue from 43rd to 46th Streets.  The Times Square Business Improvement District, which joined in the effort, deployed its faux owl _ real owls being pigeon predators _ in a planter between 45th and 46th Streets on Broadway, to convince the birds that they could get mugged in Times Square.  Giving the history behind Operation Stool Pigeon, Lucian Chalfen, a spokesman for the Department of Sanitation, said:  ``Basically, the overall complaint was that there seems to have been a proliferation of pigeons in the general Times Square area directly attributable to the fact that there are people feeding these birds. And I guess it gets around in bird circles. A nominal problem all around the city and in many cities exacerbated itself in that area.''  Christine Sheppard, curator of birds at the Bronx Zoo, said that when birds that normally go exploring for food find a good source, they have no reason to look elsewhere: ``The number of animals that come to this party continues to increase, and nobody leaves.''  Dr. Sheppard held out scant hope for the efficacy of the stone owl. ``Hilarious,'' she said. ``These birds are not stupid. In order to survive in the wild, pigeons have to be aware of predators. Any bird that sits still for 24, 48, 96 hours they know is not dangerous.''  _NEW YORK TIMES NEWS SERVICE  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Mickey Mantle's prognosis is described as excellent. Another home run would be nice, but we bet he'd settle for a home walk.  Forecasters expect several major hurricanes this season. This will be disappointing to folks hoping to keep the high winds, damage and all-wet people confined to Washington.  ``We are on the verge of having a 40-year low in the minimum wage,'' Mr. Clinton says. And who knows which office-holder after 1996 will be forced to get a job paying that?  ``Everybody wants to cut Medicaid to shreds,'' the president says. Not so. Some folks want to cut it to smaller pieces than shreds.  The man at the next desk says you know it's a bad day when you fax an e-mail to someone's voice mail but it gets detoured through the copier and into the shredder.  Newt Gingrich is going to do a cross-country tour to sell his new book, ``To Renew America.'' A cross country. That pretty well describes the U.S. now.  Any day now we expect to see an official ballot that lists the candidates' names followed by a Universal Price Code for each.  A new survey shows coin collectors would like to see Ronald Reagan's face on a proposed new dollar coin. Because they admire his mettle?  ``The mining law of 1872 needs to be modernized,'' says Mr. Clinton. Though he failed to suggest who should get the shaft.  ``Farming,'' Mr. Clinton says, ``has become more and more capital-intensive.'' It's one more way farming resembles life. Which causes us to wander out into the night, stare at the sky and imagine beautiful people with strikingly high intelligence living on some distant planet _ people who never cut their nails in public, who never order the wrong wine with dinner, who always manage to balance their checkbooks.  But my guess is that if NASA convinces Congress to fund its search for life and if we eventually find other people, we're going to be disappointed.  What, after all, ever matches our dreams? Well, I mean besides the time I dreamed I was drowning and woke up with my head sticking out of a tent in the rain.  Probably the people we find, if any, will want to lend us money at exorbitant rates. Or sell us second-quality cars at first-quality prices. Or borrow our mowers and not return them. Shoot, for people like that we can limit our search to a couple of ZIP Codes I could name but won't. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950610.0163 type=story > <HEADLINE> COMMENTARY: GOP GANGSTA RAP </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-RICH-COLUMN-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199506.gz"

Du Pont, former presidential candidate, had asserted that the Republican Political Action Committee, which he founded and over which Gingrich presided until last May, represents the ``small businessman on Main Street America.''  ``Isn't this a little ironic for you,'' Talbot asked, ``since you come from one of the oldest and most prosperous American families?''  ``Well, it's, I suppose, ironic,'' answered du Pont. ``I do come from a family that has been here for almost 200 years. But after all, my ancestors started a very dangerous gunpowder business in 1802, and my great grandfather and his father were both killed in gunpowder explosions. So, there were some entrepreneurial du Ponts that are a little different from the heads of corporations today.''  P.S. A Gingrich angle that was edited (for time) from the ``Frontline'' profile: Kit Gingrich, Newty's talkative mother _ she's the one who whispered to Connie Chung her son's opinion of Hillary Clinton _ was equally unshy when she talked of her son and his first wife, Jackie.  ``I am sorry that they divorced,'' she said. ``I really am. Because she did without a lot of things to help Newty. She tutored him, she took care of the children. They managed apartments in Atlanta. And she didn't have nice furniture ...  ``But we often wonder if she would have lost some weight if it would have helped. She was quite heavy.''  BEST AND WORST IN THE WORLD?  The new Corruption Index created by Transparency International of Berlin measures the effect of bribery on awarding government contracts in 41 countries, says Earth Island Journal.  The least corrupt countries are New Zealand, Denmark and Singapore.  The most corrupt are China and Indonesia.  The United States is 14th least corrupt, slightly less corrupt than France and Japan.  `THE FRONT PAGE' COMES TO LIFE  At a Washington performance of ``The Front Page'' on Monday night, to raise money for a local theater group's community outreach program:  _ Commentator Mark Shields, columnist Jack Germond, New York Times Washington ace R.W. Apple and ABC's Sam Donaldson were reporters playing cards in the press gallery; Ben Bradlee, retired from the Washington Post, played a police reporter.  _ Rep. Susan Molinari, R-N.Y., played a cleaning woman; Barney Frank, D-Mass., played a hoodlum; Dick Armey, R-Texas, played a corrupt sheriff.  _ Cokie Roberts played the fiance of reporter Hildy Johnson.  _ Labor Secretary Robert Reich played the death row escapee hidden in a desk; Health and Human Services Secretary Donna Shalala played a compulsively neat reporter; NAACP President Kweisi Mfume played her pal.  _ Despite past professional differences, Senator Alan Simpson, R-Wyo., as a messenger, and NPR's Nina Totenberg, as a prostitute, sang ``A Fine Romance'' to each other.  When the show was over, Donaldson said, ``I haven't had this much fun since Watergate!''  HOW THE SHRINKS STAND UP IN COURT  In its 1995 session, the New Mexico legislature pondered and passed a bill that set limits on the testimony of psychologists in court. Senate bill 459, written by Richard Romero, included the following language, quoted in the newsletter Dispatches:  ``When a psychologist or psychiatrist testifies during a defendant's competency hearing, the psychologist or psychiatrist shall wear a cone-shaped hat that is not less than two feet tall. The surface of the hat shall be imprinted with stars and lightning bolts.  ``Additionally, a psychologist or psychiatrist shall be required to don a white beard ... and shall punctuate crucial elements of his testimony by stabbing the air with a wand.'' Before the expert's testimony about competency, the bill specified, ``the bailiff shall contemporaneously dim the courtroom lights and administer two strikes to a Chinese gong.''  Although the Senate passed the bill by a voice vote, and the House voted 46 to 14 to make it official, New Mexico Gov. Gary Johnson vetoed it. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960130.0516 type=story > <HEADLINE> JOHN KILLILEA: BASKETBALL WAS HIS LIFE </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-BKO-RYAN-COLUMN-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199601.gz"

Du Pont, former presidential candidate, had asserted that the Republican Political Action Committee, which he founded and over which Gingrich presided until last May, represents the ``small businessman on Main Street America.''  ``Isn't this a little ironic for you,'' Talbot asked, ``since you come from one of the oldest and most prosperous American families?''  ``Well, it's, I suppose, ironic,'' answered du Pont. ``I do come from a family that has been here for almost 200 years. But after all, my ancestors started a very dangerous gunpowder business in 1802, and my great grandfather and his father were both killed in gunpowder explosions. So, there were some entrepreneurial du Ponts that are a little different from the heads of corporations today.''  P.S. A Gingrich angle that was edited (for time) from the ``Frontline'' profile: Kit Gingrich, Newty's talkative mother _ she's the one who whispered to Connie Chung her son's opinion of Hillary Clinton _ was equally unshy when she talked of her son and his first wife, Jackie.  ``I am sorry that they divorced,'' she said. ``I really am. Because she did without a lot of things to help Newty. She tutored him, she took care of the children. They managed apartments in Atlanta. And she didn't have nice furniture ...  ``But we often wonder if she would have lost some weight if it would have helped. She was quite heavy.''  BEST AND WORST IN THE WORLD?  The new Corruption Index created by Transparency International of Berlin measures the effect of bribery on awarding government contracts in 41 countries, says Earth Island Journal.  The least corrupt countries are New Zealand, Denmark and Singapore.  The most corrupt are China and Indonesia.  The United States is 14th least corrupt, slightly less corrupt than France and Japan.  `THE FRONT PAGE' COMES TO LIFE  At a Washington performance of ``The Front Page'' on Monday night, to raise money for a local theater group's community outreach program:  _ Commentator Mark Shields, columnist Jack Germond, New York Times Washington ace R.W. Apple and ABC's Sam Donaldson were reporters playing cards in the press gallery; Ben Bradlee, retired from the Washington Post, played a police reporter.  _ Rep. Susan Molinari, R-N.Y., played a cleaning woman; Barney Frank, D-Mass., played a hoodlum; Dick Armey, R-Texas, played a corrupt sheriff.  _ Cokie Roberts played the fiance of reporter Hildy Johnson.  _ Labor Secretary Robert Reich played the death row escapee hidden in a desk; Health and Human Services Secretary Donna Shalala played a compulsively neat reporter; NAACP President Kweisi Mfume played her pal.  _ Despite past professional differences, Senator Alan Simpson, R-Wyo., as a messenger, and NPR's Nina Totenberg, as a prostitute, sang ``A Fine Romance'' to each other.  When the show was over, Donaldson said, ``I haven't had this much fun since Watergate!''  HOW THE SHRINKS STAND UP IN COURT  In its 1995 session, the New Mexico legislature pondered and passed a bill that set limits on the testimony of psychologists in court. Senate bill 459, written by Richard Romero, included the following language, quoted in the newsletter Dispatches:  ``When a psychologist or psychiatrist testifies during a defendant's competency hearing, the psychologist or psychiatrist shall wear a cone-shaped hat that is not less than two feet tall. The surface of the hat shall be imprinted with stars and lightning bolts.  ``Additionally, a psychologist or psychiatrist shall be required to don a white beard ... and shall punctuate crucial elements of his testimony by stabbing the air with a wand.'' Before the expert's testimony about competency, the bill specified, ``the bailiff shall contemporaneously dim the courtroom lights and administer two strikes to a Chinese gong.''  Although the Senate passed the bill by a voice vote, and the House voted 46 to 14 to make it official, New Mexico Gov. Gary Johnson vetoed it. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960131.0121 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``l'' lifestyle file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1996 San Francisco Chronicle  THE DU PONT LEGACY  Pierre (Pete) du Pont IV, cousin of John du Pont, who was arrested this week and charged with the fatal shooting of wrestler David Schultz, talked about his family when he was interviewed by Steve Talbot recently for the recent ``Frontline'' biography of Newt Gingrich that was shown on PBS. OLY-BOXING-ART _ AUGUSTA, Ga. _ After a worrisome start that featured more bellyaching than body punching, the U.S. boxing team breaks camp here this morning and heads to Atlanta brimmimg with confidence and sounding like a cohesive unit. What remains to be seen is how tough they are.  OLY-ARCHERY _ ATLANTA _ Citing his failure to attend a crucial competition in Germany and a weeklong training camp in Atlanta, five of the six U.S. Olympic archers asked their coach to resign, team members said Thursday. Mike King of Grand Rapids, Mich., the U.S. coach, confirmed the request, but said he refused because of his commitment to the National Archery Association.  OLY-FLEE _ ATLANTA _ A second athlete is missing from his training camp at Reinhardt College, a Gambian runner who apparently has since been seen in Atlanta, a school spokeswoman said Thursday.  OLY-DUCKEK _ CONYERS, Ga. _ Hermann Duckek is a connoisseur of soil and sand. And he has spent the past month in Conyers stirring, sifting and directing tractors and workmen to create a scientifically engineered equestrian carpet of dirt. Duckek is the world's undisputed master of ``footing,'' the dirt surface on which horses perform. He has supervised the footing at every Olympic Games since 1976, except Los Angeles in 1984.  OLY-TWIGG-ART _ undated _ She started college at age 14. Now, at 33, she is preparing to start graduate school in the fall. In between, she won seven national cycling championships and six world titles. She became a computer programmer, and she found God. This is Rebecca Twigg. World-class athlete. Intellectual. Developing spiritual being. Complete person.  OLY-PROTEST _ undated _ Cyclist Clara Hughes will represent the Canadian Olympic team in the road race and individual time trial, but she may be remembered for an event in which she won't compete.  OLY-GWEN _ CHAPEL HILL, N.C. _ Less than a month ago, Gwen Torrence was being widely billed as the lead female attraction at the Olympics in her hometown. Since then, she fell short of the Olympic squad in her favorite event, the 200 meters. Slowed by injury, she was nudged out by upstart Inger Miller for third place, the final qualifying spot. Her troubles weren't over. But she hasn't shed a tear.  OLY-DREAM-ART-2takes _ undated _ Comfortably ensconced in the pool room at the Dream Team's resort hotel in Scottsdale, Ariz., head coach Lenny Wilkens sat down with Cox News Service's Ailene Voisin for a question/answer session.  OLY-SAIL _ SAVANNAH, Ga. _ Andy Zawieja did what anyone might as Hurricane Bertha veered from the Olympic yachting venue Thursday. He shopped for a washer and dryer.  OLY-CYCLING _ undated _ Jeanne Golay has a recurring nightmare: The Olympic road race is tomorrow, and she's not ready. Suddenly, she wakes with a fright, gathers her thoughts and realizes her race isn't until July 21. Even as a dream, it is a fitting metaphor for U.S. Olympic cycling.  OLY-HANDBALL _ undated _ You won't find a team handball assembly line churning out players and stamping them with Made in the USA labels. So how does a country, where the market craves baseball, basketball and football, produce a top-of-the-line handball player? It exports.  OLY-WRESTLE _ CHATTANOOGA, Tenn. _ Gordy Morgan knows a thing or two about big things. He's from a big family, loves hunting big game, likes a big truck with big tires and is big-time humble. The humility comes easily when you're the 10th of 12 kids. You learn your place _ and you get the bed in the basement. And share it with your baby brother.  OLY-KAYAK _ DUCKTOWN, Tenn. _ It was one of those moments of true world friendship the Olympics is supposed to be all about. American whitewater kayaker Scott Shipley, a medal favorite, gave a $2,000 kayak to a Bosnian paddler who is just  lucky to be here after four years of war in his homeland.  OLY-HUMMER-COLUMN _ undated _ In Kip Keino's world, pride is the most valuable currency. He is certain it will carry the body farther and lift the spirit higher than mere coin of the realm.  OLY-OLDERATHLETES _ undated _ Athletes escape the grasp of Father Time a while longer.  OLY-OLDER-MAINTAIN _ undated _ Five athletes tell how they've changed their training habits to turn back time and maintain their competitive edge. OLY-HORSES _ ATLANTA _ Horse- drawn carriages, banned downtown during the Olympics for fear that horses and shoulder-to-shoulder crowds wouldn't mix, will be allowed to operate after all. (t)  EVERGLADES _ FLAMINGO, Fla. _ Everglades National Park covers the southern end of the Florida peninsula, a 2,343-square-mile sweep of sawgrass plains, mangrove islands and subtropical hammocks. The third largest national park in the country _ only Denali in Alaska and Yellowstone in Wyoming are bigger _ is home to about 300 kinds of birds, almost 1,000 species of flowering plants, 65 kinds of reptiles, alligators and crocodiles, and 14 endangered species including the Florida panther, the leatherback turtle and the manatee. PHOTOGRAPHER'S EX-WIFE SENTENCED FOR THEFT OF HIS WORK (bl) By MONTE WILLIAMS c.1996 N.Y. Times News Service  WHITE PLAINS, N.Y. _ The former wife of O. Winston Link, the 81-year-old photographer known for his black-and-white images of America's last railroad steam engines, was sentenced here Tuesday to 61/3 to 20 years in prison for stealing more than $1 million worth of his photographic prints.  Early last month, Conchita Link, 61, was found guilty of grand larceny in a case that captured the attention of the photography and art world. In contrast to the allegations of thievery and betrayal, Mr. Link's photographs captured the romance of the rails of the 1950s and America's erstwhile innocence.  The sentence imposed by Judge Daniel Angiolillo of Westchester County Court, considered harsh by law-enforcement officials, was not tough enough for Mr. Link. He read a long statement urging the court to give his ex-spouse the maximum sentence, 81/3 to 25 years. He said she would scream ``have a heart attack'' in hopes he would die and leave behind his valuable artwork.  ``In my 81 years,'' he said, ``I have never met nor heard of anyone more evil than her.''  Mrs. Link, who did not testify during the trial, said before sentencing that she was responsible for Mr. Link's fame because of her efforts to market his photos. She said she did not know the whereabouts of the prints, adding: ``Where would I go to sell these prints? I'm known in the art world.''  The judge admonished Mrs. Link: ``You took advantage of someone who trusted you. You took advantage of someone's age.''  In 1992, Mr. and Mrs. Link began lobbing charges of physical and psychological abuse at each other. At the trial here, gallery owners testified that Mrs. Link falsely led them to believe that her husband was suffering from Alzheimer's disease so that she could take over his business affairs.  Mrs. Link's lawyer, Darien J. Zoppo, did not call any witnesses or provide jurors with any clear version of events.  But in an earlier civil case that included a divorce, Mr. Link won damages of more than $5 million after Mrs. Link made several admissions that proved damaging in the criminal case.  For example, she admitted writing a letter asserting that she was the legal owner of all of Mr. Link's work after Mr. Link had formally notified her in 1992 that she would have no further input in his business affairs.  During the criminal trial, Mr. Link said he saw his ex-spouse packing what appeared to be boxes of the prints in her car. Although Mr. Link said he has negatives of the 1,400 missing prints of steam locomotives of the the Norfolk &AMP; Western Railway, estimated to be worth $1.6 million dollars, he said his ability to oversee the production of new prints may be impaired because of his age.  District Attorney Jeanine Pirro said her office would prosecute anyone caught selling or buying the missing prints. ``I caution anyone if they are approached about buying the prints: We will continue to prosecute this matter.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960820.0642 type=story > <HEADLINE> HIT THE LOTTERY, BUY A TOASTER: MUNDANE ADS REPLACE HYPE </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ALBANY, N.Y. Times News Service  HIGH COURT HEARS CASE AGAINST U.S.-REQUIRED ADS  The Supreme Court, which has long agreed that businesses have a First Amendment right to advertise their products, heard the other side of the advertising coin: whether businesses may decline to take part in a Government-required ad program.  A group of handlers of California peaches, nectarines and plums sued, claiming a First Amendment right not to engage in Government-compelled speech, and refused to pay fees assessed by the Department of Agriculture to urge consumers, for example, to ``buy California peaches.'' The fees can run to a half million dollars for a big farm and total more than $10 million a year.  ECONOMY SHOWN GETTING STRONGER  Economic strength was shown by two measures _ in contrast to other recent data that showed a cooling economy. The National Association of Purchasing Management's index rose to 52.7, and construction spending rose 1.8 percent in October.  DOW UNCHANGED AS NASDAQ GAINS  Stock prices were mixed, with the Dow Jones industrial average unchanged at 6,521.70. It had an early slide of nearly 49 points after the strong economic news raised inflation fears, but bargain hunters drove stock prices back up. The Nasdaq index set its sixth straight record, rising 7.21 points, to 1,299.82, as computer-related issues gained.  Treasury prices fell, pushing the yield on the 30-year bond up to 6.36 percent.  VW IGNORES GM IN PROMOTION  Ignoring a General Motors ultimatum, Volkswagen has promoted a former GM executive who GM had demanded be dismissed in the legal dispute between the two companies. Volkswagen named Francisco Javier Garcia Sanz to take the seat on the company's managing board vacated by Jose Ignacio Lopez de Arriortua, left, the former G.M. executive who was forced to step down at VW last week.  MERRILL LYNCH NAMES PRESIDENT  Merrill Lynch selected Herbert M. Allison Jr. as president and chief operating officer, making him the likely eventual successor to David H. Komansky, who is to succeed Daniel P. Tully, as the company's head.  VORNADO'S LURE: $50 MILLION  Michael Fascitelli quit his lucrative partnership at Goldman, Sachs to become president of Vornado Realty Trust for a pay package worth at least $50 million.  JEEPS DRIVE CHRYSLER SALES UP  Chrysler sales rose 1.4 percent last month, in line with analysts' expectations, as the wildly popular sport utility vehicles, minivans and pickup trucks gained 7.3 percent, to 127,527. Car sales fell 10.6 percent, to 52,101.  USERS OVERWHELM ON-LINE SERVICE  Computer users eager to take advantage of America Online's new flat-rate pricing plan, which includes unlimited Internet access, overwhelmed the system, causing widespread busy signals across the nation.  GERMAN COMMENT BOLSTERS DOLLAR  The dollar surged to nearly two-year highs of 1.5536 German marks and 1.3220 Swiss francs. Signs of robust American economic growth helped it, as did comments by Hans Tietmeyer, Germany's central bank chief, who suggested that the dollar should rise further.  DISCRIMINATION BY CIRCUIT CITY FOUND  A federal jury found that Circuit City Stores had discriminated against black employees in promotions at its headquarters. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19961202.0787 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``a'' national news file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1996 San Francisco Chronicle  YOU ORDER EGG ROLLS, I'LL GET FRIES  Conceptual artist Don Celender's latest question to 300 prominent Americans: ``If you could dine with a famous artist from the past, or present, without budgetary limits, whom would you select and where would you dine?''  The response rate was 82 percent; the original questionnaires are on display at the O.K. Harris Gallery in New York this month.  The most popular dining companion, with 10 votes, was Leonardo da Vinci. (That's counting a clever vote from AIDS researcher Robert Gallo, who said he would want to dine with Michelangelo at Leonardo's house). Psychologist Albert Ellis was among those who weren't particular about the venue. ``Any decent restaurant with low-fat unsalted food would do!'' he wrote.  Alan Alda wanted to dine with Rembrandt in Amsterdam at ``a great rice table restaurant''; Helen Gurley Brown chose Matisse, and he could choose the place; Sandra Day O'Connor selected Berthe Morisot ``in a good Paris bistro''; P.J. O'Rourke named Yoko Ono. ``We'd go to one of those restaurants in Japan where they serve poisonous blowfish,'' he wrote. ``I'd order a burger.''  Desi Arnaz Jr. wanted to dine with Norman Rockwell at Carlos Mexican restaurant in Boulder City, Nev.; newsman Bernard Shaw named El Greco, who has ``always been my man,'' in front of the church wall that bears his masterpiece, `The Burial of Count Orgaz' ''; painter Larry Rivers wanted to eat with Caravaggio ``in a gay bath house''; Tom Wolfe suggested ``a picnic on a rock just offshore of the island of North Haven, Maine, at 5:30 some September afternoon with Winslow Homer.''  Then there was Nancy Kerrigan, who said she would dine with Walt Disney ``at a restaurant of his choice''; and Maynard Ferguson, who wanted to have lunch with Paul Gauguin ``at the Hyatt Regency in Tahiti.''  Gentlemanly cartoon artist Robert Crumb said that his wife, artist Aline Kominsky, was first choice. ``My second choice would be, I suppose, Brueghel the Elder. I'd like to find out if he was really as sharp a dude as I think he was. ... It'd be very interesting to take him to some modern-day restaurant like Taco Bell or some typical pizzeria and watch his cold contempt build for our age.''  GIVING  _ In a Bazaar story wherein posh designers revealed their favorite Christmas presents _ to give, to get, to crave _ Clodagh, a designer who seems to go by one name, suggested ``life-enhancing gifts, like a lifetime supply of favorite scented candles.'' Salami, perhaps.  _ ``The concept was to make a Jewish celebration beer,'' Jeremy Cowen, inventor of He'brew, the Chosen Beer, told the Jewish Bulletin. The beer costs about $3 a bottle, shopping information is available at (415) 3-HEBREW, and its slogan, ho, ho, ho, is ``Don't pass out, Pass-over.''  _ In the interests of medicine rather than bathroom humor, it should be noted that makers of UltraTech products, who produce the Flatulence Filter Seat Cushion (enough about that), claim that: ``British University research shows increase in cancer from breathing secondhand flatulence gas.''  _ Brigitte Ziano, head personal shopper at F.A.O. Schwarz in San Francisco, explained to Paul Kilduff of the Monthly why the Eat at Ralph's game is priced at half-off. The game, as Kilduff describes it, ``barfs food back out at you.''  ``The parents were not quite happy with that,'' said Ziano, ``because it was quite messy. And, as you know, all the kids like it when it's messy. The more it's messy the better they like it.''  HOW TO RAISE RATS WHO ARE HAPPY AS CLAMS  Canadian researchers announced last week that rats tended by loving and attentive mothers are more adventurous and can handle stress better than other rats.  Stress hormone levels in the loved rats were much lower than in the unloved rats, and they calmed down more quickly after reacting to stress.  The McGill University researchers, whose work was reported in the New Scientist, found that there was naturally a wide range of care levels among rats. The most loving mothers expressed their caring by nursing, grooming and licking their offspring. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19961202.0788 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``l'' lifestyle file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1996 San Francisco Chronicle  YOU ORDER EGG ROLLS, I'LL GET FRIES  Conceptual artist Don Celender's latest question to 300 prominent Americans: ``If you could dine with a famous artist from the past, or present, without budgetary limits, whom would you select and where would you dine?'' It's a matter of opinion, I suppose, but I am fairly clear in my mind about two key criteria for books to be included in a column that runs once a month and must come in at under 1,000 words. "99730"	"I"	"suppose"	" Story Filed By Cox Newspapers </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20011103.0126 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-POLITICAL-BRIEFS-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> BALTIMORE MAYOR BUCKS TREND; WAR OF WORDS IN MINNESOTA AND SOUTH DAKOTA; NAME RECOGNITION SHAPES ILLINOIS RACE By B. DRUMMOND AYRES JR. c.2001 New York Times News Service  For almost two years, most talk about the 2002 governor's race in Maryland has centered on Lt. Gov. Kathleen Kennedy Townsend.  Townsend has been an active, high-profile No. 2, a good start for running for the No. 1 spot. She has been all over the state meeting and greeting and raising money to run. And as one of Robert F. Kennedy's daughters, she has the political lineage to run.  No big surprise, then, that with those credentials she has scared off the other major Democrats who would be governor _ except Mayor Martin O'Malley of Baltimore.  O'Malley, now almost halfway through his term, has not classified himself as a Democratic hopeful for governor. But he talks a lot about the job and the need for a ``real leader'' to fill it.  The broad assumption is that he thinks Kennedy is not that leader but that he could be _ or could be a major player in the effort to find that leader, increasing his and his city's political influence in Annapolis, the capital.  O'Malley has not directly attacked Townsend. Mostly he has left it to others to speak resentfully of how, because Republicans seldom do well in statewide races in heavily Democratic Maryland, she has been all but anointed as the next governor. But last week, pressed by The Baltimore Sun on where he stood on the race, O'Malley offered this:  ``I'm waiting to see some leadership from people who want to be governor, and so far I haven't see a whole lot of that. There are those that feel there's a big vacuum in this race. Any number of people could fill that vacuum, I suppose, including the one known candidate. But so far I think that most people would agree there is a pretty big vacuum.''  Neighborly Repartee Takes On an Edge  Minnesota and South Dakota share a border, and their governors, like good neighbors, talk to each other a lot. The exchanges have been mostly good-natured, but lately they have taken on an edge _ which was probably inevitable, given that few governors are more outspoken than Jesse Ventura of Minnesota, an independent, and William J. Janklow of South Dakota, a Republican.  It all began two weeks ago when Ventura, at a trade show in Canada, speculated that Janklow was not at the show because he was home ``cutting new ads'' to induce Minnesotans and Minnesota businesses to move to South Dakota.  Then, warming to his topic, Ventura added: ``I love his ads, and I love the points he makes. The bottom-line problem is you then have to live in Sioux Falls. If that element wasn't in there, you'd be home free.''  Sioux Falls, just west of Minnesota's southwest corner, has received laudatory livability reviews in recent years in several polls and business publications. Janklow immediately countered the governor, a former pro wrestler, with this smackdown: ``Whoever said that sounds like a jerk.''  Then last week, after a few days of quiet, Janklow went back on the offensive.  A Minnesota company had decided to expand _ but in South Dakota, he announced. He said he wanted Ventura to know ``how pleased we are that another company has seen that their future in Minnesota means an investment in the people of South Dakota.''  Ventura responded that the company would not stay long in South Dakota after it discovered how much higher Janklow taxes were than Ventura taxes.  Stay tuned.  Name Recognition Shapes Illinois Race  Election Day is still a year off for the 2002 Illinois governor's race. So how is it shaping up this early?  A new Chicago Tribune poll indicates that name recognition is about the biggest factor for Republican and Democratic candidates.  On the Democratic side, Roland W. Burris, a former state attorney general, holds a two-to-one lead over the four other hopefuls: Paul Vallas, the former head of Chicago public schools; Rep. Rod R. Blagojevich, from Chicago's North Side; Michael J. Bakalis, a former state comptroller; and Patrick Quinn, a former state treasurer. Ninety percent of poll respondents knew who Burris was, while no other candidates had name recognition above 75 percent.  Still, Burris has little reason to be content with his initial numbers. Four in 10 voters said they were undecided this far from the election. In two previous races for governor, name recognition also put Burris out front in early polls, but he finished second in both contests.  Among Republican hopefuls, the poll found Attorney General Jim Ryan, with almost 100 percent name recognition, leading better than three to one over the two other hopefuls, Lt. Gov. Corinne Wood and state Senator Patrick O'Malley from suburban Chicago. Their name recognition was around 80 percent. The undecided vote was almost 25 percent.  The poll had other good news for Ryan. It found he had strong support in both the moderate wing of the party, Wood's base, and the conservative wing, O'Malley's base.  No Democrat-Republican matchups were included in the survey. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20011103.0127 type=story > <HEADLINE> SPORTS COLUMN: OH, THOSE HATED YANKEES </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> NEW YORK (BC-BBO-LIPSYTE-COLUMN-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200111.gz"

"112809"	"I"	"suppose"	" The MacLiammoir (and now, I suppose, the Callow) form of storytelling theater is different: it is about the audience surrendering to its own imagination, which then in turn feeds back to the storyteller. The power of suggestion is all, and every audience will respond with its own and different mental pictures, creating a unique sense of possibility in the auditorium. In this form of theater, too, where different characters are assumed one after another without recourse to makeup or change of costume, there is an element of complicity in the art of acting that is unexpectedly exhilarating. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200204.gz"

NY-AUSTRIAN-ARCHITECTURE (New York) _ In architecture, the history of ideas is more reliable than the history of forms. It was almost worth suffering through postmodernism to absorb this simple lesson. Visually similar forms can convey appreciably different meanings. Not all glass elevations deliver the same message as that presented by the landmarks of the International Style. In the case of the new Austrian Cultural Forum in Manhattan, the message is quite contrary. In contrast to the lucid rationality of the modern glass tower, the forum projects the idea that serious disturbances may lie beneath a relatively smooth appearance. Call this a psycho-building: every skyline goes a little crazy sometimes. By Herbert Muschamp.  NOGUCHI-EXHIBIT (New York) _ By no means an artist of narrow focus, the Japanese-American sculptor Isamu Noguchi (1904-1988) took Zen Buddhism into his creative vocabulary, along with Western concepts and sensibilities. Although he was never a Zen practitioner, he admired the discipline's encouragement of spareness and simplicity, its stress on meticulous craftsmanship, and its insistence on asymmetry, or lack of formal balance, to turn the mind away from excess rationalism. Noguchi's take on Zen, a somewhat Westernized version of it, is on view in ``Zen No Zen: Aspects of Noguchi's Sculptural Vision,'' a selection of works from the Isamu Noguchi Garden Museum in Long Island City, which is undergoing renovation. By Grace Glueck.  DARGER-OUTSIDE-ART (New York) _ ``Right or wrong, to be different is to be distinguished,'' wrote the American artist Henry Darger (1892-1973), who was different and distinguished indeed. He was 5-foot-1 and talked to himself more than to anyone else. He was raised in an asylum for ``feeble-minded'' children but wrote, and lavishly illustrated, a fantasy novel more than 15,000 pages long. He died in obscurity in Chicago; today he is one of the stars in an international constellation of ``outsider'' artists. He is also a 21st-century culture hero. The American Folk Art Museum in Manhattan, after acquiring the bulk of the manuscripts and drawings, has created the Henry Darger Study Center for the scholarly elucidation of his impoverished life and protean work. In fact, his life and work are inseparable. Most artists and writers are daily commuters to the imagination. Darger lived in it full time. By Holland Cotter.  MILLIE-THEATER-REVIEW (New York) _ The opening of ``Thoroughly Modern Millie,'' adapted from the 1967 movie musical of the same title. Reviewed by Ben Brantley.  CARRAFA-DANCE (New York) _ John Carrafa is a choreographer who seems to be everywhere at once. To start, he has two musicals on Broadway. ``Into the Woods,'' in previews at the Broadhurst Theater, opens on April 30. The zany hit ``Urinetown'' is two blocks away at the Henry Miller. And there is more. Carrafa is creating new choreography for ``The Pajama Game'' for performances next month in the City Center Encores! series. He will soon begin work for the Broadway-bound musical ``Dance of the Vampires.'' He continues to coach actors for dance sequences in film. He is choreographing a revival in July of ``A Little Night Music,'' part of the Sondheim Celebration at the Kennedy Center in Washington. And he will also choreograph ``Amour,'' based on the music of Michel Legrand, which is to open on Broadway in October. An interview by Jennifer Dunning.  NY-GARGOYLES (New York) _ New York may be known for its skyscrapers, but you don't have to look up that far to glimpse a less imposing, and in some ways more enticing, puckish feature of this city's architecture: its gargoyles. Whether carved from sandstone or terra cotta, these decorative monsters stand guard over buildings great and small, blending the medieval with the modern and sprinkling Gotham with a touch of the Gothic. By Thane Rosenbaum. OTHER ENTERTAINMENT  TIBET-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) _ The Tibet depicted in Alai's epic novel ``Red Poppies'' is not a place populated by meditative lamas and Buddhist pilgrims but a violent, feudal land of warring chieftains and clans. Set in the first half of the 20th century, the novel depicts a region that looked east to Peking, rather than west to Lhasa, and focuses on an era that came to a close with the Chinese occupation in the 1950s. The book was awarded China's highest literary award, the Mao Dun prize, in 2000. Reviewed by Michiko Kakutani.  TV-WARNING-REVIEW (Undated) _ ``Warning: Parental Advisory.'' Reviewed by Julie Salamon.  THEATER-NOTES (Undated) _ News of The Great White Way. By Jesse McKinley.  ANTIQUES-COLUMN (New York) _ The Antiques Column. By Wendy Moonan. MISSISSIPPI-BLUES (Undated) _ On the blues trail with Rick Bragg in the Mississippi Delta: I came looking for the blues. I came to hear a big woman rattle the windows with a voice like a hurricane spinning at the bottom of a well, to hear a skinny man sing about big-legged women and unworthy husbands, to eat cornbread and collards, to have a gospel choir on the radio snatch me up in the rapture as I speed through the green, flat country. I came to see this land where the blues began, and to see a child who was born here pick up a guitar and plug in a hot amplifier with a sound like bacon spitting from a hot skillet, and play me a song. By Rick Bragg.  With photos.  SECOND-HOMES (Undated) _ Appalachia is not yet the new Florida, but it could be making a run for it. Over the last decade, some of the fastest growth in seasonal and second homes has come not along the sunny shores of Florida or in the lush oases of the California desert. Rather, the new destinations are more likely to be in states like West Virginia, Tennessee and Arkansas, landlocked and mountainous, at least in part, but with more temperate climates than the traditional mountain retreats in the northeastern United States and the Rockies. And while second homes are still going up in more traditional vacation spots like Arizona and New Mexico, it is the less populated farther reaches of those states where growth is fastest. By Edward Wyatt.  MUSTANG-FEVER (Undated) _ Brad Barnett, 23, runs two Web sites for devotees of the Ford Mustang and is a member of the Magic City Mustangs, a Birmingham, Ala. ``We're not trying to save the world,'' said Barnett. ``It's just guys who like Mustangs, talking Mustangs.'' Today, though, the muscle cars Barnett and his fellow auto enthusiasts covet are a dying breed. The latest casualty came when General Motors said this would be the last year for its Camaro and Firebird models. Now the Mustang stands practically alone. In fact, in recognition of its staying power, in 2004, the Ford Motor Co. will move production of the car from the aging Dearborn, Mich., assembly plant to a newer facility. And J. Mays, Ford's chief designer, plans a retro look for it by mid-decade, so that its body more resembles the sinuous brute Steve McQueen drove in ``Bullitt.'' By Danny Hakim.  RITUALS-CLOSETS (Undated) _ Most people find the weekend chore of cleaning out the closet dreary, frustrating and worthy of first-degree procrastination. I find it relaxing. It gives me a sense of completion and, I suppose, control. Well-organized closets make me feel like I inhabit a well-organized world, while the truth is anything but. Rarely does a weekend pass by without finding me deep in a closet, weeding out life's debris. By Nancy M. Better.  BUYING-COMIC-BOOKS (New York) _ I read comic books. That was hard to admit when I was a kid because at school, reading comics was something only nerds did. Being out about it could get a person beaten up. It was also something my family didn't like, because they didn't approve of the ``wasted'' money. At 30, my comic collecting still feels like a dirty little secret. I am still buying comics. By George Gene Gustines.  FLOWER-POT-COOKING (Undated) _ My kitchen is not exactly powered by flowerpots, but I could make a strong case for their essential culinary life. As multifaceted kitchen enablers, nothing can quite supplant the gorgeous, timeless, ever available, often dirt-cheap terra-cotta flowerpot. By Suzanne Hamlin.  DETROIT-36-HOURS (Undated) At first glance, Detroit still looks like a city under siege. But these days the scene is not one of crime; it is one of construction. Scaffolding silhouettes the sky in the form of a new corporate office tower, a performing arts center and a professional football stadium, which is set to open in September and will play host to the 2006 Super Bowl. They are tangible examples of the $10 billion that has been spent during the last three years refurbishing the downtown area as Detroit looks to reclaim its heritage as one of the liveliest centers for music, dance and theater in the United States. The city is a compact, easily accessible place to spend a weekend break. By Jennifer Conlin.  CAR-COLORS (Undated) _ The big car color for the model year of 2004 and beyond and how it is chosen. By Tim Moran.  For information and repeats call the News Service at 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927. Thor Heyerdahl  OBIT-HEYERDAHL (Undated) _ Thor Heyerdahl, the Norwegian anthropologist and adventurer who won acclaim navigating the Pacific, Atlantic and Indian oceans to advance his controversial theories of ancient seafaring migrations, died Thursday. Heyerdahl, who was 87, died of cancer in Italy, where he had been vacationing, the family said. By John Noble Wilford.  With photo.  Editors, all obituaries carry an ``a'' general news category code.  WASHINGTON (``w'' code)  IRAN-HOSTAGES (Washington) _ A U.S. District Court judge ruled Thursday that the Americans who were held for 444 days at the U.S. Embassy in Tehran cannot win damages from Iran because the agreement that freed them 21 years ago barred any subsequent lawsuits against the Iranian government. The former hostages had won their case by default, because Iran failed to defend itself, and were seeking damages when the State Department stepped in to have the case dismissed. By Matthew L. Wald. NYT News Service  INTERNATIONAL _ (Moved in ``i'' category)  POPE-PRIEST-ABUSE (Rome) _ Cardinal Stafford, one of three American cardinals at the Vatican, talks about what will be on the table in for discussions in June _ as much as anything, it will be a dialogue between cultures, particularly the Vatican trying to understand American tort law, and privacy vs. secrecy issues. By Melinda Henneberger.  MIDEAST-ISRAEL-FAMILIES (Jerusalem) _ Interviews with five Israeli families who have lost children, siblings, parents, in recent suicide attacks, as well as with psychologists and counselors. What emerges is a complex portrait of people searching for meaning in their loss, while struggling to cope with their grief and fear. By Joel Brinkley. 1,800 words. NATIONAL _ GENERAL (Moved in ``a'' category)  GAY-PRIESTS (Undated) _ On gay men and the priesthood, and how to make sense of various incomplete and conflicting studies and reports about the sexual activity, orientation and misdeeds of priests. With the Roman Catholic church weathering one case after another of priests who sexually abused boys, the Catholic faithful are asking whether there is a closeted culture of homosexuality in the priesthood, and if so whether it is connected to the sexual abuse problem. By Laurie Goodstein. 2,000 words.  OBIT-HEYERDAHL (Undated) _ Thor Heyerdahl, the Norwegian anthropologist and adventurer who won acclaim navigating the Pacific, Atlantic and Indian oceans to advance his controversial theories of ancient seafaring migrations, died Thursday. Heyerdahl, who was 87, died of cancer in Italy, where he had been vacationing, the family said. By John Noble Wilford.  (ART ADV: Photo NYT21 is being sent to NYT photo clients.)  BUSINESS DAY _ (Moved in ``f'' category)  DRUG-WOES (Undated) _ This should be the golden age for pharmaceutical scientists. The deciphering of the human genome is laying bare the blueprint of human life. Robots and computers are turning drug discovery from a mixing of chemicals in a test tube to an industrialized, automated process. Yet if industrialization normally means higher speed and lower costs, the pharmaceutical industry has been experiencing the opposite _ a ``clear fall in productivity,'' said Frank L. Douglas, the chief scientific officer of Aventis. By Andrew Pollack.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphics clients.)  NORRIS-COLUMN (Undated) _ The weekly column. By Floyd Norris. Most of the outside directors are French executives wrestling with the the same cultural and economic conflicts that Messier has faced in overhauling Vivendi. The company also announces today that it has sold a publisher of trade magazines for 1.2 billion euros ($1 billion). By John Tagliabue. BKN-ARATON-COLUMN (New York) _ Sports of The Times column on the Knicks and Latrell Sprewell. Sprewell's bad judgment in Miami last weekend, when he ended up being fined $125,000 for missing a practice and showing up late for the game, only underlines his limitations as a supposed team leader. By Harvey Araton.  ENTERTAINMENT (Moved in ``e'' category)  NY-AUSTRIAN-ARCHITECTURE (New York) _ In architecture, the history of ideas is more reliable than the history of forms. It was almost worth suffering through postmodernism to absorb this simple lesson. Visually similar forms can convey appreciably different meanings. Not all glass elevations deliver the same message as that presented by the landmarks of the International Style. In the case of the new Austrian Cultural Forum in Manhattan, the message is quite contrary. In contrast to the lucid rationality of the modern glass tower, the forum projects the idea that serious disturbances may lie beneath a relatively smooth appearance. Call this a psycho-building: every skyline goes a little crazy sometimes. By Herbert Muschamp.  NOGUCHI-EXHIBIT (New York) _ By no means an artist of narrow focus, the Japanese-American sculptor Isamu Noguchi (1904-1988) took Zen Buddhism into his creative vocabulary, along with Western concepts and sensibilities. Although he was never a Zen practitioner, he admired the discipline's encouragement of spareness and simplicity, its stress on meticulous craftsmanship, and its insistence on asymmetry, or lack of formal balance, to turn the mind away from excess rationalism. Noguchi's take on Zen, a somewhat Westernized version of it, is on view in ``Zen No Zen: Aspects of Noguchi's Sculptural Vision,'' a selection of works from the Isamu Noguchi Garden Museum in Long Island City, which is undergoing renovation. By Grace Glueck.  DARGER-OUTSIDE-ART (New York) _ ``Right or wrong, to be different is to be distinguished,'' wrote the American artist Henry Darger (1892-1973), who was different and distinguished indeed. He was 5 feet 1 inch and talked to himself more than to anyone else. He was raised in an asylum for ``feeble-minded'' children but wrote, and lavishly illustrated, a fantasy novel more than 15,000 pages long. He died in obscurity in Chicago; today he is one of the stars in an international constellation of ``outsider'' artists. He is also a 21st-century culture hero. The American Folk Art Museum in Manhattan, after acquiring the bulk of the manuscripts and drawings, has created the Henry Darger Study Center for the scholarly elucidation of his impoverished life and protean work. In fact, his life and work are inseparable. Most artists and writers are daily commuters to the imagination. Darger lived in it full time. By Holland Cotter.  MILLIE-THEATER-REVIEW (New York) _ The opening of ``Thoroughly Modern Millie,'' adapted from the 1967 movie musical of the same title. Reviewed by Ben Brantley.  CARRAFA-DANCE (New York) _ John Carrafa is a choreographer who seems to be everywhere at once. To start, he has two musicals on Broadway. ``Into the Woods,'' in previews at the Broadhurst Theater, opens on April 30. The zany hit ``Urinetown'' is two blocks away at the Henry Miller. And there is more. Carrafa is creating new choreography for ``The Pajama Game'' for performances next month in the City Center Encores! series. He will soon begin work for the Broadway-bound musical ``Dance of the Vampires.'' He continues to coach actors for dance sequences in film. He is choreographing a revival in July of ``A Little Night Music,'' part of the Sondheim Celebration at the Kennedy Center in Washington. And he will also choreograph ``Amour,'' based on the music of Michel Legrand, which is to open on Broadway in October. An interview by Jennifer Dunning.  NY-GARGOYLES (New York) _ New York may be known for its skyscrapers, but you don't have to look up that far to glimpse a less imposing, and in some ways more enticing, puckish feature of this city's architecture: its gargoyles. Whether carved from sandstone or terra cotta, these decorative monsters stand guard over buildings great and small, blending the medieval with the modern and sprinkling Gotham with a touch of the Gothic. By Thane Rosenbaum. ESCAPES (Moved in ``l'' category)  MISSISSIPPI-BLUES (Undated) _ On the blues trail with Rick Bragg in the Mississippi Delta: I came looking for the blues. I came to hear a big woman rattle the windows with a voice like a hurricane spinning at the bottom of a well, to hear a skinny man sing about big-legged women and unworthy husbands, to eat cornbread and collards, to have a gospel choir on the radio snatch me up in the rapture as I speed through the green, flat country. I came to see this land where the blues began, and to see a child who was born here pick up a guitar and plug in a hot amplifier with a sound like bacon spitting from a hot skillet, and play me a song. By Rick Bragg.  (ART ADV: Photo and map are being sent to NYT photo and graphic clients.)  SECOND-HOMES (Undated) _ Appalachia is not yet the new Florida, but it could be making a run for it. Over the last decade, some of the fastest growth in seasonal and second homes has come not along the sunny shores of Florida or in the lush oases of the California desert. Rather, the new destinations are more likely to be in states like West Virginia, Tennessee and Arkansas, landlocked and mountainous, at least in part, but with more temperate climates than the traditional mountain retreats in the northeastern United States and the Rockies. And while second homes are still going up in more traditional vacation spots like Arizona and New Mexico, it is the less populated farther reaches of those states where growth is fastest. By Edward Wyatt.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  MUSTANG-FEVER (Undated) _ Brad Barnett, 23, runs two Web sites for devotees of the Ford Mustang and is a member of the Magic City Mustangs, a Birmingham, Ala. ``We're not trying to save the world,'' said Barnett. ``It's just guys who like Mustangs, talking Mustangs.'' Today, though, the muscle cars Barnett and his fellow auto enthusiasts covet are a dying breed. The latest casualty came when General Motors said this would be the last year for its Camaro and Firebird models. Now the Mustang stands practically alone. In fact, in recognition of its staying power, in 2004, the Ford Motor Co. will move production of the car from the aging Dearborn, Mich., assembly plant to a newer facility. And J. Mays, Ford's chief designer, plans a retro look for it by mid-decade, so that its body more resembles the sinuous brute Steve McQueen drove in ``Bullitt.'' By Danny Hakim.  RITUALS-CLOSETS (Undated) _ Most people find the weekend chore of cleaning out the closet dreary, frustrating and worthy of first-degree procrastination. I find it relaxing. It gives me a sense of completion and, I suppose, control. Well-organized closets make me feel like I inhabit a well-organized world, while the truth is anything but. Rarely does a weekend pass by without finding me deep in a closet, weeding out life's debris. By Nancy M. Better.  BUYING-COMIC-BOOKS (New York) _ I read comic books. That was hard to admit when I was a kid because at school, reading comics was something only nerds did. Being out about it could get a person beaten up. It was also something my family didn't like, because they didn't approve of the ``wasted'' money. At 30, my comic collecting still feels like a dirty little secret. I am still buying comics. By George Gene Gustines.  FLOWER-POT-COOKING (Undated) _ My kitchen is not exactly powered by flowerpots, but I could make a strong case for their essential culinary life. As multifaceted kitchen enablers, nothing can quite supplant the gorgeous, timeless, ever available, often dirt-cheap terra-cotta flowerpot. By Suzanne Hamlin.  DETROIT-36-HOURS (Undated) At first glance, Detroit still looks like a city under siege. But these days the scene is not one of crime; it is one of construction. Scaffolding silhouettes the sky in the form of a new corporate office tower, a performing arts center and a professional football stadium, which is set to open in September and will play host to the 2006 Super Bowl. They are tangible examples of the $10 billion that has been spent during the last three years refurbishing the downtown area as Detroit looks to reclaim its heritage as one of the liveliest centers for music, dance and theater in the United States. The city is a compact, easily accessible place to spend a weekend break. By Jennifer Conlin.  CAR-COLORS (Undated) _ The big car color for the model year of 2004 and beyond and how it is chosen. By Tim Moran.  (Editors: All clients receive all budgets, but only full-service clients receive all stories. Please check your level of service to determine which stories you will receive.)
