NATIONAL  CONGRESS -- WASHINGTON-- A bill to create a Homeland Security Department could pass in the Senate as soon as Friday, but it could also be held up by efforts to re-instate an independent commission to investigate intelligence failures that led up to the Sept. 11 attacks. A previous version of the bill included the commission. In addition, Sen. Robert Byrd urged lawmakers to wait a few days before voting for the bill (Moscoso, Cox News Service)  DEMLEADERS -- WASHINGTON -- Congressional Democrats elected Nancy Pelosi to be their leader today, making the liberal Californian the first woman to head either party in either chamber of Congress. Democrats chose Rep. Steny Hoyer of Maryland, a 21-year-veteran known for working well with Republicans, to succeed Pelosi as whip, the party's second-highest rank. Rep. Robert Menendez of New Jersey, a leader of the Hispanic caucus, was elected caucus chairman. (Shepard, Cox News Service)  WITH  PELOSI -- WASHINGTON-- A profile of Rep. Nancy Pelosi, the new minority leader. (Dart, Cox News Service)  WITH  DEM-BIOS -- Thumbnails of the elected party leaders. (Shepard, Cox News Service)  TER-HOSPITALS -- WASHINGTON-- Hospitals in Washington and three other cities are being warned about possible terrorist threats, the latest in a series of alerts about possible attacks during the Muslim Ramadan holy month. It also coincides with the execution today of a Pakistani man convicted of killing several CIA workers. (Aird, Cox News Service)  CLIMATE-STATES -- WASHINGTON-- A new report from the Pew Center on Global Climate Change details innovative approaches implemented by the several states, including Georgia, to mitigate climate change. (Nesmith, Cox News Service)  EMORY-PRES -- ATLANTA -- Emory University President William M. Chace announced today he will retire at the end of the school year. Chace told the board of trustees he plans to teach at Emory after a year's sabbatical. Chace, 64, has been Emory's president since 1994. (Ghezzi, The Atlanta Journal-Constitution)  INTERNATIONAL  IRAQ-JOURNAL -- BAGHDAD, Iraq -- Cruising in a metallic chocolate Mercedes Benz ``Gamballa,'' I watched Baghdad's symbols of power roll past the tinted windows _ the mammoth domed mosques, the portraits of Saddam Hussein, the towering oil ministry. I was looking for the source of the song ``Sweet Home Alabama'' that sprang from my car radio on a visit to Baghdad last month. After two rough decades, it seems that many Iraqis cling to a '60s and '70s vibe. (Kaplow, Cox News Service)  INS-REGISTER -- ATLANTA -- Beginning Friday, male students, tourists and businesspeople from Iran, Iraq, Libya, Sudan and Syria must register with the INS if they arrived in the U.S. before Sept. 10, 2002. They have until Dec. 16 to come to an INS office to be fingerprinted, photographed and interviewed. Those who don't comply face deporation. (Bixler, The Atlanta Journal-Constitution)  OPED/EDITORIAL  TEEPEN-COLUMN _ New South? What New South? The Democratic governors of South Carolina and Georgia lost re-election at least in part because they had retired the old Confederate battle flag, slavery's ensign, from prominence in their states. (Tom Teepen is national correspondent for Cox Newspapers.) MOVED. The storm hit the Indian state of West Bengal on Tuesday, then headed toward Bangladesh. The densely populated coastal areas of eastern India and Bangladesh are regularly hit by storms from the Bay of Bengal.  NEPAL: AFTER STRIKE, REBELS STATE DEMANDS  At the end of a three-day national strike, leaders of the Maoist insurgency issued a statement reiterating their demands for peace. The Maoists, who have been waging a guerrilla war against the constitutional monarchy since 1996, want a conference with all parties taking part, the formation of an all-party government and an election for a constituent assembly. The government said the rebels should present their demands at a negotiating table.   EUROPE   CYPRUS: ADVICE FROM U.S. ON PEACE PLAN  A U.S. envoy urged Cyprus' rival Greek and Turkish leaders to seriously consider a new U.N. peace plan or miss out on a ``settlement opportunity that may not exist for some time to come.'' The appeal by Thomas Westen, the Bush administration's special envoy on Cyprus, came after the United Nations presented a plan this week calling for the creation of a federation on Cyprus with the Greek and Turkish halves united by a central government modeled on Switzerland's. Resolving the dispute is seen as crucial to Cyprus' hopes to join the European Union.   YUGOSLAVIA: RE-RUNNING FOR PRESIDENT  Vojislav Kostunica, the president of Yugoslavia, announced that he would run again for the same post in Serbia, the federation's main republic. Kostunica won the Serbian presidential election last month only to see his victory annulled because less than half the electorate voted, largely because of disillusionment with political infighting among the reformers who toppled Slobodan Milosevic. Kostunica has since struck a deal with his main rival, the Prime Minister Zoran Djindjic of Serbia, but it is unclear whether he will have Djindjic's support in the election on Dec. 8.  CROATIA: WANTED GENERAL ENTERS HOSPITAL  A senior Croatian general wanted on war crimes charges by the U.N. tribunal in The Hague checked himself into a hospital in Zagreb. Janko Bobetko, an 83-year-old diabetic with a deteriorating heart condition, refuses to acknowledge the jurisdiction of the tribunal, and the Croatian government is resisting international pressure to hand him over.  CZECH REPUBLIC: PLOT TO DARKEN NATO SUMMIT  The police in North Moravia announced that they had arrested five high school and university students who were planning to cut off electricity to the NATO summit meeting in Prague next week. The police said the youths fashioned themselves as anarchists but had no links to international terrorist groups. Instead, they seemed to like to cut off electrical installations for fun, said Karel Trnka, the chief of the North Moravian criminal police.   SPAIN: WARRANT ISSUED FOR BASQUE POLITICIAN  The Supreme Court issued an international arrest warrant for a member of the Basque regional parliament who failed twice to appear in court on charges that he ordered a terrorist bombing in 1987. Jose Antonio Urruticoetxea, who was imprisoned for membership of the violent separatist group ETA and later elected to the regional parliament for the Batasuna party, which is now outlawed, told a newspaper he would not respond to the court summons because ``I was judged a long time ago.'' He is accused of ordering the ETA bombing of a Civil Guard barracks in Zaragoza in 1987, which killed 11 people, including five children.  SPAIN: LEAKING TANKER TOWED FROM COAST  An aging tanker leaking fuel oil that made a 20-mile slick off the coast of northwestern Spain was towed toward international waters on Thursday night in the hope of averting an environmental disaster. The high winds and 25-foot waves buffeting the tanker died down at midday, allowing tugs to haul the ship out to sea and enabling technicians to restart an engine. Officials said the ship would be escorted 120 miles offshore and probably turned over to a salvage company to be taken into harbor for repair.   WORLD   NO AGREEMENT ON BIO-WEAPONS INSPECTIONS  Countries meeting in Geneva to discuss strengthening the Biological Weapons Convention failed to resolve a dispute on inspections to verify compliance with the pact, which prohibits the development, production and stockpiling of the weapons. Last year, the United States blocked attempts to set guidelines to verify compliance, saying the proposals would not deter countries from acquiring or developing biological weapons. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021114.0214 type=story > <HEADLINE> WHO'S BEEN SLEEPING IN MY BED? </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 11-14-02 18:09:00EST (BC-LIVE-IN-CARETAKERS-NY) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200211.gz"

"143224"	"I"	"suppose"	" I understand the importance of closure to my family, friends, and supporters, Martha Stewart declared 10 days ago as she told the world she was ready for her stint in the slammer. She's not the only one, these days, who's aware of the importance of closure. A dozen years after the word was first denounced in print as psychobabble, its detractors say ..closure remains a blight on the lexicon of loss. Worse, some say, it is edging out closing in non-therapeutic contexts. Local TV announcers, a friend writes, are prattling on about closures of area churches. Another reader e-mails, I wonder if closings will ever be allowed back? Then, I suppose, I will have closure. But their gloom may be premature. A look at the evidence suggests that the noun form closing is holding up nicely, and closure may actually be past its peak. Before it moves on to Buzzword Heaven, though, closure deserves a closer look; its life story is more complicated than most critics have noticed. Yes, closure has clear origins in the world of psychology. It was introduced in the 1920s by Gestalt therapy, which defined it as (in the words of the Oxford English Dictionary) the process whereby incomplete forms, situations, etc., are completed subjectively by the viewer or seem to complete themselves; the tendency to create ordered and satisfying wholes. That covers everything from our ability to see a triangle even though its perimeter isn't complete to our predilection for constructing narratives that make sense of our experience. But the Gestalt version of closure remained a specialized usage till recently. It wasn't part of the pop-psych vocabulary skewered in Richard Rosen's 1977 book Psychobabble, for instance, even though Gestalt therapy was part of the cultural landscape at the time. The Age of Aquarius, for reasons of its own, was more interested in getting in touch with its inner child and going with the flow than in pursuing closure. Meanwhile, some earlier uses of closure -- as a synonym for concluding or closing -- were chugging along, meeting no opposition. And why should they? The first sense is recorded in 1588, the second in 1600, in a reference to Augustus's second closure of the temple of Janus. And when closure did break into wider usage in our time, it was the signing a deal, resolving the situation senses that made the leap, not the psychological term. Here's Secretary of State George Shultz in 1987, after a meeting in Moscow: While we have explored matters in the space-defense ABM treaty area . . . we don't see quite where we can come to closure on that subject. Or a senior US official quoted in 1991: It is quite difficult to understand how (Israelis and Palestinians) cannot come to closure. The spread of this diplomatic closure may have helped to revive the therapeutic term, or simply softened us up for it, but it certainly came first: The grief-related closure made its big move a bit later, between 1990 and 1995. Since that surge, though, its popularity has leveled off -- perhaps because of the fierce opposition it met. Just from 1997 through 1999, closure was called the psychotherapeutic cliche du jour, the cliche word of modern tragedy, and even the most reviled (term) in the English language. Further evidence that the campaign against the therapeutic-jargon closure may succeed: Of the half-dozen most recent print examples I looked at one day last week, four were direct quotes from people who hoped for (or despaired of achieving) closure, one was sarcastic, and only one was straight. If the victims of misfortune would stop talking to reporters about closure, the usage would probably go extinct. Of closure as a synonym for closing, the same cannot be said. In current newspapers, in fact, this version of closure dominates: The word is roughly three times as likely to refer to lane closings or station closings as to emotional resolution. And since this usage is 400 years old, we may as well grin and bear it; otherwise, closure could be a long time coming. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200409.gz"

Photo and graphic notes will be added on Friday. If you have questions aboutand performance remained muddy -- at best.  EXEC-PENSIONS (Undated) -- When Vance D. Coffman retired as chief executive of Lockheed Martin last August, the company wanted to show a little gratitude. So the board endowed a $1.5 million professorship at Iowa State University, Coffman's alma mater, to honor his steady leadership over 37 years. It also agreed to foot the bill for various expenses, like his country club membership and use of company aircraft, until he steps down as chairman this month at the age of 61. Those perks, however, are pocket change compared with the pension that Lockheed bestowed upon Coffman a few weeks ago: a $31.5 million lump-sum payment. Everyone knows that chief executives are paid huge amounts of money while they are working. Less known is just how much they make in retirement. At many of America's biggest corporations, it is not uncommon for retired executives who were paid tens or even hundreds of millions of dollars during their tenures to receive $1 million or more in pension benefits every year -- for as long as they live. By Eric Dash.  Other Exec Pay stories  EXEC-BONUSES (Undated) -- It is hard to find three chief executives who had a tougher year than Carley Fiorina, Raymond V. Gilmartin and Michael D. Eisner. But you would never guess that from the size of their annual bonuses. At Hewlett-Packard, Fiorina was forced to step down after directors concluded that her strategy wasn't working. But the board reinstated part of her bonus after a strong fourth quarter, so she still managed to pocket $1.57 million. At Merck, Gilmartin collected a $1.4 million bonus for 2004, despite the fact that Vioxx, its top-selling pain medicine, was pulled from the shelves late last year; investors pulled out of the company's stock even faster. At Disney, Eisner was the subject of a shareholder revolt that led him to retire a year earlier than he originally said. No matter. He received a $7.25 million bonus for the company's greatly improved fiscal performance in 2004. The fact that all three made out so well underscores a trend in executive pay last year: The big bonus is back. By Eric Dash.  EXEC-PERKS (Undated) -- Pressed by shareholder activists, companies are starting to be more forthcoming about the corporate jets, company cars and other perquisites they give their executives on top of their salaries, bonuses and other compensation. But there is scant evidence that the fuller disclosure is reining in any regal lifestyles just yet. The amounts of money spent on executive perks vary widely, and they rise and fall inexplicably. Most often, the cash goes to sweeten corporate officers' health care benefits and retirement accounts. But there other popular perks that fall outside the office, like the annual bill for the chief executive's country club membership. By Patrick McGeehan.  EXEC-PAY-OPTIONS (Undated) -- Fifteen years ago, Michael C. Jensen, a professor at the Harvard Business School, co-wrote a paper that trumpeted some pretty radical ideas for the time. Compensation systems, they posited, prompted chief executives to add revenue, not to increase profit, pay dividends or otherwise reward long-suffering shareholders. Their suggestion was to make stock options a big component of top management's pay, ensuring that they do well only if shareholders do well. Of course, it turned out that in far too many cases, stock options tempted managers to pick strategies, schedule deals and investments, even juggle the numbers, so that the company looked best when it came time to exercise those options. So in 2001, Jensen debunked his own theories in another paper. But Jensen, 65, is still not done with the subject. Claudia H. Deutsch. talks to him. In Q&amp;A form.  Commentary  MARKET-WATCH (Undated) -- Weekly Wall Street commentary. By Gretchen Morgenson.  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly Economic View column: Tax reform, like a second marriage, is the triumph of hope over experience. The United States has just gone through the most sweeping tax cuts since the 1980s, but hardly anybody is satisfied. President Bush contends that we need still-lower taxes in order to prosper. Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, is suggesting a radical shift to a consumption tax. And the Republican Party has taken aim at the entire tax system. At the heart of all this antitax sentiment is a belief: Taxes are bad for the economy. There is just one problem, though. Despite the widespread notion that taxes harm the economy, no one has actually been able to back that up. By Anna Bernasek.  BILLIONAIRES (Undated) -- The On the Contrary column: This year's Forbes list of the world's billionaires is out, and, as usual, the news is bad. You didn't make it. Neither did I. You may have fantasized about what it would be like to have multiple homes, to cruise around on your yacht and spend time counting your cash. But even if billionaire status never arrives, allow me to set your yearnings at ease. Do not envy these people. For most of the superrich, the benefits of each additional dollar of wealth reached the vanishing point long ago. I can't speak from experience, of course, but in all likelihood, having billions does not offer significant advantages over having mere millions and indeed imposes the disadvantage of some significant cost burdens. By Daniel Akst.  Features  AIRFARES (Undated) -- Five years ago, flying across the United States was much the same as it had been throughout much of the jet age. Passengers booked a ticket on one of the big airlines, like United or American, and paid an average of $600 round-trip in coach class unless they snagged a discount. But low-fare airlines have been gobbling market share throughout the industry, and they are now focusing on the transcontinental market with a vengeance. Cross-country flights account for almost half the business that the low-fare companies have added during this decade. As a result, average ticket prices have plummeted, turning the transcontinental market, like those for flights within California or from East Coast cities to Florida -- into a big air-fare flea market. By Micheline Maynard.  Investing  INVEST-BONDS (Undated) -- Longer-term interest rates may finally be moving up in earnest. If the trend continues, fixed-income investors who have not prepared will be taught an ugly lesson: as yields rise, prices fall and the value of a bond portfolio declines. But there are ways to minimize the pain. The most common moves are to sell longer-term bonds and to hold cash or shorter-term securities, which won't be hurt as badly as their cousins with longer maturities. Some portfolio managers also use futures contracts to hedge against rate increases. But professionals are also using a less known way to cushion a bond portfolio: by buying premium callable bonds. By Virginia Munger Kahn.  AUTOS-KIA (Undated) -- A review of the Kia Spectra 5, a pleasant, economical and surprisingly stylish hatchback/wagon. Move over, Maybach: This $15,000 Korean car gets compliments from valet attendants at chic L.A. nightspots. By Michelle Krebs.  AUTOS-KOREANS (Undated) -- The Korean automakers were bottom feeders not long ago, but now they are nipping at the heels of the Japanese -- and have rising Chinese companies closing in from behind. That is why the Koreans are expanding their footprint in the United States, and are moving into new market segments. For instance, a new large sedan from Hyundai, the 2006 Azera, looks like a serious challenger to the Toyota Avalon and Nissan Maxima, especially with more luxury features and a lower price. By Michelle Krebs.  For Monday release:  CARS-AMERICAN-MOTORS (Undated) -- Pacers, Marlins and Gremlins, oh my! It also puts governance of the religion of more than a billion believers into a state of suspended animation. By Daniel J. Wakin.  EXP-POPE-VIGIL (Dateline TK) -- Around the world, Roman Catholics and others offered prayers on Friday for the gravely ill Pope John Paul II. (Byline TK)  EXP-ZIMBABWE-ELECT (Harare, Zimbabwe) -- Nearly complete results in Zimbabwe's national parliamentary elections pointed on Friday to a victory for President Robert G. Mugabe's ruling party and a drubbing for his political opposition, which had predicted a dramatic showing. Some democracy advocates here called for the opposition to mount mass protests against the Mugabe government, saying this week's vote proved that a fair election was currently impossible. By Sharon LaFraniere and Michael Wines.  EXP-IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- A group of Sunni Arab clerics, including some hard-line figures who fiercely oppose the American presence in Iraq, issued a statement on Friday urging Iraq's Sunni Arabs to join the Iraqi army and police. The edict was a striking turnaround for the clerics, who have often lashed out in sermons at the fledgling army and police and branded them collaborators. By Robert F. Worth.  EXP-MEXICO-MAYOR (Mexico City) -- A congressional panel here on Friday passed a motion to strip Mayor Andres Manuel Lopez Obrador of Mexico City, the country's most popular politician, of his official immunity so he can stand trial in a land dispute. The move could prevent him from running in the upcoming presidential election. By Ginger Thompson.  EXP-SPAIN-TERROR (Madrid, Spain) -- The Spanish police took 13 people into custody on Friday in connection with the deadly train bombings here last year, including a man extradited from Belgium who is accused of being one of al-Qaida's top leaders in Europe, the Interior Ministry said. By Renwick McLean.  EXP-PHARMACY-BIRTH-CONTROL (Chicago) -- With a growing number of reports of pharmacists around the country refusing to fill prescriptions for birth control and emergency contraception, Gov. Rod R. Blagojevich on Friday filed a rule requiring pharmacies to promptly accept and dispense all such prescriptions in Illinois. By Monica Davey.  EXP-GUNS-DEBATE (Undated) -- Across the country, efforts to expand or establish laws allowing concealed handguns have been fueled by the horrifying shootings in the last month. By Kate Zernike.  EXP-ECON-REPORT (Washington) -- The nation's unemployment rate dipped slightly in March, the government said on Friday, but job creation was weaker than most analysts had expected. The nation added about 110,000 jobs last month, about half what Wall Street analysts had predicted, as the unemployment rate declined to 5.2 percent from 5.4 percent in February. By Edmund L. Andrews. THE NEW YORK TIMES NEWS SERVICE </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20050401.0127 type=advis > <HEADLINE> SUNBIZ-FINAL-BUDGET-NYT </HEADLINE> <TEXT> Editors, updates with photo and graphic notes, adds two inside stories, MORGAN-STANLEY and LEVERAGED-BUYOUTS. The New York Times News Service will move the following business features and commentary for release Sunday, April 2. If you have questions about stories, call (888) 346-9867 or (212) 556-1927. For questions about photos, call (888) 603-1036 or (212) 556-4204. On the Cover EXEC-PAY (Undated) -- The spectacle of once-respected corporate titans doing perp walks -- Martha Stewart, Bernard J. Ebbers, Richard M. Scrushy, the list seems endless -- has pretty well tarnished the title of chief executive. But it has done little, it seems, to scratch the gilt from the corner office. In fact, the boss enjoyed a hefty raise last year. The chief executives at 179 large companies that had filed proxies by last Tuesday -- and had not changed leaders since last year -- were paid about $9.84 million, on average, up 12 percent from 2003, according to Pearl Meyer &amp; Partners, the compensation consultants. Surely, chief executives must have done something spectacular to justify all that, right? Well, that's not so clear. The link between rising pay and performance remained muddy -- at best.and performance remained muddy -- at best. With a graphic.  EXEC-PENSIONS (Undated) -- When Vance D. Coffman retired as chief executive of Lockheed Martin last August, the company wanted to show a little gratitude. So the board endowed a $1.5 million professorship at Iowa State University, Coffman's alma mater, to honor his steady leadership over 37 years. It also agreed to foot the bill for various expenses, like his country club membership and use of company aircraft, until he steps down as chairman this month at the age of 61. Those perks, however, are pocket change compared with the pension that Lockheed bestowed upon Coffman a few weeks ago: a $31.5 million lump-sum payment. Everyone knows that chief executives are paid huge amounts of money while they are working. Less known is just how much they make in retirement. At many of America's biggest corporations, it is not uncommon for retired executives who were paid tens or even hundreds of millions of dollars during their tenures to receive $1 million or more in pension benefits every year -- for as long as they live. By Eric Dash.  EXEC-BONUSES (Undated) -- It is hard to find three chief executives who had a tougher year than Carley Fiorina, Raymond V. Gilmartin and Michael D. Eisner. But you would never guess that from the size of their annual bonuses. At Hewlett-Packard, Fiorina was forced to step down after directors concluded that her strategy wasn't working. But the board reinstated part of her bonus after a strong fourth quarter, so she still managed to pocket $1.57 million. At Merck, Gilmartin collected a $1.4 million bonus for 2004, despite the fact that Vioxx, its top-selling pain medicine, was pulled from the shelves late last year; investors pulled out of the company's stock even faster. At Disney, Eisner was the subject of a shareholder revolt that led him to retire a year earlier than he originally planned. No matter. He received a $7.25 million bonus for the company's greatly improved fiscal performance in 2004. The fact that all three made out so well underscores a trend in executive pay last year: The big bonus is back. By Eric Dash.  With a photo, NYT18.  EXEC-PERKS (Undated) -- Pressed by shareholder activists, companies are starting to be more forthcoming about the corporate jets, company cars and other perquisites they give their executives on top of their salaries, bonuses and other compensation. But there is scant evidence that the fuller disclosure is reining in any regal lifestyles just yet. The amounts of money spent on executive perks vary widely, and they rise and fall inexplicably. Most often, the cash goes to sweeten corporate officers' health care benefits and retirement accounts. But there other popular perks that fall outside the office, like the annual bill for the chief executive's country club membership. By Patrick McGeehan.  With a graphic.  EXEC-PAY-OPTIONS (Undated) -- Fifteen years ago, Michael C. Jensen, a professor at the Harvard Business School, co-wrote a paper that trumpeted some pretty radical ideas for the time. Compensation systems, they posited, prompted chief executives to add revenue, not to increase profits, pay dividends or otherwise reward long-suffering shareholders. Their suggestion was to make stock options a big component of top management's pay, ensuring that they do well only if shareholders do well. Of course, it turned out that in far too many cases, stock options tempted managers to pick strategies, schedule deals and investments, even juggle the numbers, so that the company looked best when it came time to exercise those options. So in 2001, Jensen debunked his own theories in another paper. But Jensen, 65, is still not done with the subject. Claudia H. Deutsch. talks to him. In Q&amp;A form.  MARKET-WATCH (Undated) -- Weekly Wall Street commentary: American International Group is no Enron. Of that we can be sure. AIG, after all, is a real company with global operations, generating genuine profits from a variety of financial enterprises. As companies go, Enron was all smoke and mirrors. Moreover, until five minutes ago, AIG was run by Maurice R. Greenberg, a brilliant man who was both visionary and micromanager, a far cry from the know-nothing that Ken Lay, Enron's former chief executive, claims to be, now that his trial looms. There are, however, some disturbing similarities between AIG and Enron: Asleep-at-the-switch auditors. Secretive off-balance-sheet entities that should have been included on the company's financial statements but weren't. A management team willing to try any number of accounting tricks to make the company's results appear better than they actually were. And one more likeness: As AIG's shares have plummeted, the financial position of one of the company's insurance subsidiaries has taken a big hit. By Gretchen Morgenson.  With a graphic.  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly Economic View column: Tax reform, like a second marriage, is the triumph of hope over experience. The United States has just gone through the most sweeping tax cuts since the 1980s, but hardly anybody is satisfied. President Bush contends that we need still-lower taxes in order to prosper. Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, is suggesting a radical shift to a consumption tax. And the Republican Party has taken aim at the entire tax system. At the heart of all this antitax sentiment is a belief: Taxes are bad for the economy. There is just one problem, though. Despite the widespread notion that taxes harm the economy, no one has actually been able to back that up. By Anna Bernasek.  BILLIONAIRES (Undated) -- The On the Contrary column: This year's Forbes list of the world's billionaires is out, and, as usual, the news is bad. You didn't make it. Neither did I. You may have fantasized about what it would be like to have multiple homes, to cruise around on your yacht and spend time counting your cash. But even if billionaire status never arrives, allow me to set your yearnings at ease. Do not envy these people. For most of the superrich, the benefits of each additional dollar of wealth reached the vanishing point long ago. I can't speak from experience, of course, but in all likelihood, having billions does not offer significant advantages over having mere millions and indeed imposes the disadvantage of some significant cost burdens. By Daniel Akst.  LEVERAGED-BUYOUTS (Undated) -- The Dealbook column: How many leveraged buyout firms does it take to do a deal? Evidently, quite a few. Last week, seven of the nation's most prominent private equity firms crowded into one deal, teaming up to buy SunGard Data Systems for $11.3, the largest leveraged buyout since Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &amp; Co. bought RJR Nabisco in 1989 for $25 billion. Now Wall Street is aflutter with talk of still bigger leveraged buyouts with even bigger teams of private equity firms. But as LBO deals get bigger -- and, more important, the number of private equity firms in a club or consortium starts to bulge to pay the escalating prices -- problems are sure to emerge. By Andrew Ross Sorkin.  Features  MORGAN-STANLEY (New York) -- On the surface, the open revolt by eight retired Morgan Stanley executives against the leadership of Philip J. Purcell seems to be a power struggle, pure and simple, over who should control that storied investment house. In a larger sense, however, the attempted putsch may represent a final death rattle of a bygone Wall Street era, personified by the well-born, Ivy League-educated investment bankers who formed the core of Morgan Stanley during its heyday in the 1970s and 1980s. By Landon Thomas Jr.  AIRFARES (Undated) -- Five years ago, flying across the United States was much the same as it had been throughout much of the jet age. Passengers booked a ticket on one of the big airlines, like United or American, and paid an average of $600 round-trip in coach class unless they snagged a discount. But low-fare airlines have been gobbling market share throughout the industry, and they are now focusing on the transcontinental market with a vengeance. Cross-country flights account for almost half the business that the low-fare companies have added during this decade. As a result, average ticket prices have plummeted, turning the transcontinental market, like those for flights within California or from East Coast cities to Florida -- into a big air-fare flea market. By Micheline Maynard.  INVEST-BONDS (Undated) -- Longer-term interest rates may finally be moving up in earnest. If the trend continues, fixed-income investors who have not prepared will be taught an ugly lesson: as yields rise, prices fall and the value of a bond portfolio declines. But there are ways to minimize the pain. The most common moves are to sell longer-term bonds and to hold cash or shorter-term securities, which won't be hurt as badly as their cousins with longer maturities. Some portfolio managers also use futures contracts to hedge against rate increases. But professionals are also using a less known way to cushion a bond portfolio: by buying premium callable bonds. By Virginia Munger Kahn.  AUTOS-KIA (Undated) -- A review of the Kia Spectra 5, a pleasant, economical and surprisingly stylish hatchback/wagon. Move over, Maybach: This $15,000 Korean car gets compliments from valet attendants at chic L.A. nightspots. By Michelle Krebs.  With photos, NYT29-30, and a graphic.  AUTOS-KOREANS (Undated) -- The Korean automakers were bottom feeders not long ago, but now they are nipping at the heels of the Japanese -- and have rising Chinese companies closing in from behind. That is why the Koreans are expanding their footprint in the United States, and are moving into new market segments. For instance, a new large sedan from Hyundai, the 2006 Azera, looks like a serious challenger to the Toyota Avalon and Nissan Maxima, especially with more luxury features and a lower price. By Michelle Krebs.  CARS-AMERICAN-MOTORS (Undated) -- Pacers, Marlins and Gremlins, oh my! People who cant forget the sometimes forgettable (and occasionally endearing) cars of American Motors. By John Matras.  CARS-WAYNES-WORLD (Undated) -- The AMC Pacer used in the Wayne's World movies was recently sold on eBay. Who bought it? Why? A short, by John Matras. LA-SCALA-MUTI (Undated) -- Even by operatic standards, the plot turned with stunning swiftness. Not two months ago, Riccardo Muti seemed the unshakable master of the Teatro Alla Scala, the legendary opera house in Milan. Italy. But now, besieged in the political arena and denounced by the orchestra he so lovingly molded over two decades, he has all but lost his grip on the company. New twists arise almost daily. How long will Muti, 63, who is as proud and stubborn as he is brilliant and charismatic, continue to weather insults intended to force his departure from the position he has held since 1986? By Daniel J. Wakin and James R. Oestreich.  MCDONAGH-THEATER (New York) -- Acclaimed by critics on both sides of the Atlantic, Martin McDonagh remains a confounding character: A writer of enormous lyrical gifts and sometimes off-putting arrogance; a mild-mannered, soft-spoken vegetarian whose plays depict casual torture, buckets of blood and the occasional decapitation; and a self-described skeptic of the magic of theater who has nonetheless become one of the most successful and produced playwrights in the world. By Jesse McKinley.  HIRST-PAINTING (New York) -- With his first New York show since 2000, Damien Hirst, abandoning his famous menagerie of dead sharks and pharmaceuticals and 8-foot-wide ashtrays, has unveiled 31 photorealist paintings, all heavily assistant-aided and tongue in cheek. The subjects include skulls, crystals, pills, a doctor proffering a brain. The show's opening galvanized the predictable mob of ebullient celebrities and tut-tutting rubberneckers. By Michael Kimmelman.  Editors, Frank Rich, whose column anchored the Arts &amp; Leisure cover, has moved back to the Sunday Op-Ed page. His once-a-week columns will begin there on April 10.  Theater  DENZEL-WASHINGTON-BROADWAY (New York) -- During his working vacation away from Hollywood, Denzel Washington may be willing to go without the luxury trailer, but the bodyguard stays: a very large bearded man leads the way to the small room in the Belasco Theater where Washington, in a long-sleeved T-shirt and jeans, is relaxing before afternoon rehearsal for Julius Caesar. The show is still in previews, but in the corner, there's already a bin full of letters from fans who have seen the show; the theater had to hire extra security to handle the mob that waits for him each night after the curtain falls. Playing Brutus, Washington is adjusting to the high-wire act of live theater -- and yes, he says, it's a lot more work than film. Susan Dominus talked to him. In Q&amp;A format.  Film  CHOW-FILM (Undated) -- Previous generations of Hong Kong action heroes have flown through the air with the aid of hidden wires and trampolines. But now, Hong Kong filmmakers are following Hollywood into the digital age, using computer technology to manipulate images and make the feats of their protagonists seem even more otherworldly and astonishing. One of the comic (and even poetic) highlights of Stephen Chow's new film Kung Fu Hustle finds Chow leaping so far off the ground that he encounters Buddha in the form of a cloud formation, before he plunges back to earth to deliver a devastating coup de grace to an opponent. At 42, he is East Asia's most popular comedy star, having appeared in more than 50 films since his big screen debut in 1988. Chow is now looking for new worlds to conquer, both creatively as a writer and director and commercially as a box-office name in the Western hemisphere. By Dave Kehr.  Dance  AMERICAN-DANCE (Undated) -- Look at the rosters of important American ballet companies. At the Boston Ballet, 17 of 20 of the top dancers are foreign-born and at least initially foreign trained. In San Francisco, only four of the 17 principals were born in this country. American Ballet Theater has long welcomed foreign-trained stars. The same pattern prevails in many other big companies. In our era of globalization, is this a problem, or merely a swing of the pendulum from internationalism to nativism and back again? By John Rockwell.  Art and Architecture  ART-PRIZES (Undated) -- For decades now, Robert Rauschenberg has held the highest artistic honors: for starters, pride of place on the walls of institutions like MoMA, the Guggenheim and the Tate. But recently the artist fielded a more obscure accolade: the Premio Internacional Julio Gonzalez, one of a multitude of new art prizes awarded by the museum world to living artists. Over the last few years, museums large and small have started awarding their own prizes, usually named after the institution and sponsored by a corporate donor, to capitalize on the glamour associated with contemporary art. To burnish their appeal, many of the new awards are modeled on the Tate Modern's venerable Turner Prize, which has evolved into a nationally televised event that attracts celebrity presenters like Madonna and habitually polarizes the British press. By Douglas Heingartner.  Bestseller Lists  BESTSELLERS-HARD-L (Undated) -- Best-selling hardbacks, long-form list.  BESTSELLERS-HARD-S (Undated) -- Best-selling hardbacks, short-form list.  BESTSELLERS-PAPER-L (Undated) -- Best-selling paperbacks, long-form list.  BESTSELLERS-PAPER-S (Undated) -- Best-selling paperbacks, short-form list.  NOTEWORTHY-PAPERBACKS (Undated) -- Short summaries of other paperbacks.  On Language  ON-LANGUAGE (Undated) -- The weekly language column. By William Safire.  TRAVEL-COPPOLA-RESORTS (Undated) -- Visiting Francis Ford Coppola's resorts in Central America. By Michelle Green.  TRAVEL-KIEV (Undated) -- There is a sense of optimism that Ukraine may shake off the post-Soviet funk that left it mired in economic chaos and political intrigue. By Steven Lee Myers.  TRAVEL-WELSH-PATAGONIA (Undated) -- Argentine Patagonia is home to awe-inspiring glaciers, condors, rugged wilderness -- and a community of Welsh immigrants who have preserved the culture and language of their homeland. The center of all things Welsh is a hamlet of many gardens but no stoplights called Gaiman, where both Spanish and Welsh are likely to be heard on the street. By Luke Jerod Kummer.  TRAVEL-HONGKONG (Undated) -- The tower at the top of Victoria Peak -- Hong Kong's most-visited tourist destination -- will undergo a yearlong overhaul aimed at making it more fashionable and more Chinese. The tower is the upper terminus of a tram that has been running since 1888. By Keith Bradsher.  TRAVEL-BARGAINS (Undated) -- On the lookout for travel deals and discounts. By Pamela Noel.  EXEC-PAY (Undated) -- The spectacle of once-respected corporate titans doing perp walks -- Martha Stewart, Bernard J. Ebbers, Richard M. Scrushy, the list seems endless -- has pretty well tarnished the title of chief executive. But it has done little, it seems, to scratch the gilt from the corner office. In fact, the boss enjoyed a hefty raise last year. The chief executives at 179 large companies that had filed proxies by last Tuesday -- and had not changed leaders since last year -- were paid about $9.84 million, on average, up 12 percent from 2003, according to Pearl Meyer &amp; Partners, the compensation consultants. Surely, chief executives must have done something spectacular to justify all that, right? Well, that's not so clear. The link between rising pay and performance remained muddy -- at best. By Claudia H. Deutsch.  With a graphic.  EXEC-PENSIONS (Undated) -- When Vance D. Coffman retired as chief executive of Lockheed Martin last August, the company wanted to show a little gratitude. So the board endowed a $1.5 million professorship at Iowa State University, Coffman's alma mater, to honor his steady leadership over 37 years. It also agreed to foot the bill for various expenses, like his country club membership and use of company aircraft, until he steps down as chairman this month at the age of 61. Those perks, however, are pocket change compared with the pension that Lockheed bestowed upon Coffman a few weeks ago: a $31.5 million lump-sum payment. At many of America's biggest corporations, it is not uncommon for retired executives who were paid tens or even hundreds of millions of dollars during their tenures to receive $1 million or more in pension benefits every year -- for as long as they live. By Eric Dash.  EXEC-BONUSES (Undated) -- It is hard to find three chief executives who had a tougher year than Carley Fiorina, Raymond V. Gilmartin and Michael D. Eisner. But you would never guess that from the size of their annual bonuses. At Hewlett-Packard, Fiorina was forced to step down after directors concluded that her strategy wasn't working. But the board reinstated part of her bonus after a strong fourth quarter, so she still managed to pocket $1.57 million. At Merck, Gilmartin collected a $1.4 million bonus for 2004, despite the fact that Vioxx, its top-selling pain medicine, was pulled from the shelves late last year; investors pulled out of the company's stock even faster. At Disney, Eisner was the subject of a shareholder revolt that led him to retire a year earlier than he originally planned. No matter. He received a $7.25 million bonus for the company's greatly improved fiscal performance in 2004. The fact that all three made out so well underscores a trend in executive pay last year: The big bonus is back. By Eric Dash.  With a photo, NYT18.  EXEC-PERKS (Undated) -- Pressed by shareholder activists, companies are starting to be more forthcoming about the corporate jets, company cars and other perquisites they give their executives on top of their salaries, bonuses and other compensation. But there is scant evidence that the fuller disclosure is reining in any regal lifestyles just yet. The amounts of money spent on executive perks vary widely, and they rise and fall inexplicably. Most often, the cash goes to sweeten corporate officers' health care benefits and retirement accounts. But there other popular perks that fall outside the office, like the annual bill for the chief executive's country club membership. By Patrick McGeehan.  With a graphic.  EXEC-PAY-OPTIONS (Undated) -- Fifteen years ago, Michael C. Jensen, a professor at the Harvard Business School, co-wrote a paper that trumpeted some pretty radical ideas for the time. Compensation systems, they posited, prompted chief executives to add revenue, not to increase profits, pay dividends or otherwise reward long-suffering shareholders. Their suggestion was to make stock options a big component of top management's pay, ensuring that they do well only if shareholders do well. Of course, it turned out that in far too many cases, stock options tempted managers to pick strategies, schedule deals and investments, even juggle the numbers, so that the company looked best when it came time to exercise those options. So in 2001, Jensen debunked his own theories in another paper. But Jensen, 65, is still not done with the subject. Claudia H. Deutsch. talks to him. In Q&amp;A form.  MARKET-WATCH (Undated) -- Weekly Wall Street commentary: American International Group is no Enron. Of that we can be sure. AIG, after all, is a real company with global operations, generating genuine profits from a variety of financial enterprises. As companies go, Enron was all smoke and mirrors. Moreover, until five minutes ago, AIG was run by Maurice R. Greenberg, a brilliant man who was both visionary and micromanager, a far cry from the know-nothing that Ken Lay, Enron's former chief executive, claims to be, now that his trial looms. There are, however, some disturbing similarities between AIG and Enron: Asleep-at-the-switch auditors. Secretive off-balance-sheet entities that should have been included on the company's financial statements but weren't. A management team willing to try any number of accounting tricks to make the company's results appear better than they actually were. And one more likeness: As AIG's shares have plummeted, the financial position of one of the company's insurance subsidiaries has taken a big hit. By Gretchen Morgenson.  With a graphic.  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly Economic View column: Tax reform, like a second marriage, is the triumph of hope over experience. The United States has just gone through the most sweeping tax cuts since the 1980s, but hardly anybody is satisfied. President Bush contends that we need still-lower taxes in order to prosper. Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, is suggesting a radical shift to a consumption tax. And the Republican Party has taken aim at the entire tax system. At the heart of all this anti-tax sentiment is a belief: Taxes are bad for the economy. There is just one problem, though. Despite the widespread notion that taxes harm the economy, no one has actually been able to back that up. By Anna Bernasek.  BILLIONAIRES (Undated) -- The On the Contrary column: This year's Forbes list of the world's billionaires is out, and, as usual, the news is bad. You didn't make it. Neither did I. You may have fantasized about what it would be like to have multiple homes, to cruise around on your yacht and spend time counting your cash. But even if billionaire status never arrives, allow me to set your yearnings at ease. Do not envy these people. For most of the superrich, the benefits of each additional dollar of wealth reached the vanishing point long ago. I can't speak from experience, of course, but in all likelihood, having billions does not offer significant advantages over having mere millions and indeed imposes the disadvantage of some significant cost burdens. By Daniel Akst.  LEVERAGED-BUYOUTS (Undated) -- The Dealbook column: How many leveraged buyout firms does it take to do a deal? Evidently, quite a few. Last week, seven of the nation's most prominent private equity firms crowded into one deal, teaming up to buy SunGard Data Systems for $11.3, the largest leveraged buyout since Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &amp; Co. bought RJR Nabisco in 1989 for $25 billion. Now Wall Street is aflutter with talk of still bigger leveraged buyouts with even bigger teams of private equity firms. But as LBO deals get bigger -- and, more important, the number of private equity firms in a club or consortium starts to bulge to pay the escalating prices -- problems are sure to emerge. CAMILLA-STYLE (London) -- The bride wore tweed. Or at least you get the feeling that she would, if she could. Only the most hard-hearted could fail to sympathize, if even a teeny bit, with Camilla Parker Bowles as the days dwindle before her wedding to Prince Charles on April 8. Comparisons to the late Princess Diana, which linger deep in the British psyche, have come inevitably to the forefront. And in the fashion arena, Parker Bowles has often seemed defenseless. She has endured the scorching public eye and the jabs of style critics for years. It would be unfair, unkind and even superficial, however, to dismiss the future Duchess of Cornwall's sense of style as nonexistent. Instead, Parker Bowles's simple tailored suits and hunting-jacket-plus-jodhpurs look is that of the quintessential upper-crust British woman, her peers say. And, some add, even though she is in her late 50s, her sense of fashion is rapidly evolving. By Heather Timmons. , it seems,	 EXEC-PAY (Undated) -- The spectacle of once-respected corporate titans doing perp walks -- Martha Stewart, Bernard J. Ebbers, Richard M. Scrushy, the list seems endless -- has pretty well tarnished the title of chief executive. But it has done little, it seems, to scratch the gilt from the corner office. In fact, the boss enjoyed a hefty raise last year. The chief executives at 179 large companies that had filed proxies by last Tuesday -- and had not changed leaders since last year -- were paid about $9.84 million, on average, up 12 percent from 2003, according to Pearl Meyer &amp; Partners, the compensation consultants. Surely, chief executives must have done something spectacular to justify all that, right? Well, that's not so clear. The link between rising pay and performance remained muddy -- at best. , they posited,	 EXEC-PAY-OPTIONS (Undated) -- Fifteen years ago, Michael C. Jensen, a professor at the Harvard Business School, co-wrote a paper that trumpeted some pretty radical ideas for the time. Compensation systems, they posited, prompted chief executives to add revenue, not to increase profits, pay dividends or otherwise reward long-suffering shareholders. Their suggestion was to make stock options a big component of top management's pay, ensuring that they do well only if shareholders do well. Of course, it turned out that in far too many cases, stock options tempted managers to pick strategies, schedule deals and investments, even juggle the numbers, so that the company looked best when it came time to exercise those options. So in 2001, Jensen debunked his own theories in another paper. But Jensen, 65, is still not done with the subject. Claudia H. Deutsch. talks to him. In Q&amp;A form. </P> 	nyt_eng_200504.gz
